SATURDAY, MAY 14, 1892.
A Returned “ Loyalist.”
It is a sad and solemn reflection that Aus-
tralia has produced, at various times, perhaps
a dozen or so of the most utterly profound
political grovellers by whom the earth has
been infested this century or more. The art
of grovel cannot be altogether acquired ; it
is an inherited instinct which is afterwards
brought to perfection by much assiduous
culture and carefully acquired ignorance.
The perfect groveller is almost invariably the
man who has spent his whole life in one
narrow community, and to whom the whole
outside world is a blank. An absolutely
parochial individual has two alternatives
before him ; he becomes either a blatant and
offensive patriot who regards everything
beyond his own little sphere as utterly
mean, wretched, and beneath contempt ; or
else he developes into a servile worshinper of
foreign titles, foreign royalties, and foreign
opinions, for exactly the same reason that
the naked savage worships the thunder
because they are far off and more or less un-
known. Of this latter variety is that re-
cently returned globe-trotter, Mr. L F
Heydon, M.L.C. of New South Wales, who
last week delivered an oration before a
Sydney audience on the greatness, grandeur,
and absolute moral perfection of England’
the utter dependence of Australia upon that
far-otl island, the necessity of worshipping
it with all the mental horse-power of the
community, and the base ingratitude of
Republicanism, Federalism, and everything
that England doesn’t approve of. Mr.
Heydon is the ideal groveller. He belongs
to a narrow, soul-destroying profession ; he
is a member of a legislative body that is
bigoted, useless, and utterly selfish ; he has
never previously been outside Australia, and
all his youth and middle age have been spent
in acquiring a great, enduring ignorance of
everything beyond his own beaten track ;
and in all these ways he is eminently fitted
to be an Imperial Federationist of the worst
order. He is an Australian native - (he kind
of native which an inscrutable Providence
now and then inflicts upon every nation lest
it should become too much inflated, and his
country endures him as best it can without
repining.
* # •
Ihe central idea of Mr. Heydon’s philo-
sophy is that the British nation lives, not for
its own aggrandisement, or to accumulate
wealth for its own enjoyment, or to conquer
new lands and add them to its own terri-
tories, or to do anything whatsoever for its
own benefit— -its one desire is to help along
this continent, and all it- wants for itself is
the joy of paying the bill. Some British
confidence-man, realising that Mr. Heydon
was green and harmless, has evidently told
him in a burst of candour that Britain is the
one disinterested, pure, noble-minded, all-
wool nation upon this earth, and he has
taken it all in. Then he comes back to pour
it all out again upon his suffering audience
as follows :
.V lia b it occurred to me, are the objects abroad for
uo • Kurland strains her diplomacy and incurs all the
eav.y burdens of preparation for war? In the first place,
1,,, her diplomatic troubles at least arise over Esrypt.
Ihe national vanity of the great French nation is in-
E =-VPt and the Suez Canal. And France is
I looatrly if not certainly, the greatest military Power in
tne world She has the fleet next in importance to that
ot England, and it is from her shores alone that any in-
vasion of England could possibly succeed. Yet old Eii<«-.
lanil braves tier worst anger to maintain an absolute con-
trol over the Suez Canal. By cleverness and boldness, by
balancing the triple against the dual alliance, and by a
resolute intimation that, come what may, in no event shall
tie great waterway to the East pass out of her bands,
England holds a firm grasp on the gates of the Canal.
And for whom does she make this spirited stand ? Wbo
benefits by it? Jf Australia is ever to have a trade, if we
are to emerge (as we must, of course) from our baby state
ot slender population and feeble commerce, and take our
rank in the world as the colossal Britain of the Pacific
ami Indian Oceans, what route must our ships take to
Europe? And whom will it benefit more than us to have
i i .‘I 1 llal 'ds, absolutely free for every
h itish keel ? Would it suit us to have France or Russia
V c \ e \ ? e ! OS0 llie or overcharge our tonnage?
Aid this immense seivice old England does for us
p.oudly, independently, generously, and asks us not a
P3miy towards its great cost. What is the next object
II whicii England faces powerful foes and lavs heavy
burdens on her resources? India. And is it no advantage
o Australia that our nearest neighbour and most natural
°'stonier, a land of SOO millions of people, should be in
England s control. Should we be better off if a Russian
ukase might any moment bar all tier ports to any but
Russian trade? England’s activity in (he whole East, in
India Buniiah, perhaps Annam, and in Borneo and all
tne Indies, is of immense advantage to Australia
winning for us vast markets and most valuable customeis
in the future. As England faces France in Egypt for our
profit even more than her own, so in India she faces
Russm. Another sphere of England’s activity at present
is ttie East of Africa. We may hope that very much
indeed, if not the whole of the eastern half of that immense
continent, from the mouth of the Nile to Capetown may
ultimately be brought by English diplomacy, English
valour, L.nglish enterprise, and English money, under the
umhsputed sway of England. And here again we benefit.
If India and the East Indian isles are our nearest’neigh-
bour of all, East Africa is the next. From our position
it is immensely valuable to us that all the nearer side of
Alnca, equal to several Europes in magnitude, should be
assuredly free and open to our trade. The Suez Canal,
India and Eastern Africa, these are the objects of
England s efforts to-day, and they all concern ns even
more intimately than England. Yet sac asks us for not
one penny of aid. With her magnanimous mother’s pride
in us she pays ail and bears the burden alone.
* * #
And the sum of all this is that as Emdand
lives entirely for the sake of Australia, (here-
foie Australia should live entirely for the
sake of England in return and her one
desire should bo to be thought worthy of a
smile from that grand, old mother-land. Mr.
Heyden, like the majority of his kind, is
guiltless of history, geography, and common
sense. Ho docsn t even know, apparently,
that England started to hold India with all
her might and niatn many years before she
ever owned Australia, wherefore it may be
safely presumed she holds it now for exactly
the same reason as she did then—because
it is a profitable speculation according to the
eternal laws of her god, which is the Golden
Calf. Also, he is apparently unaware that
she holds Egypt, as everybody except Mr.
Heyden knows, as a bailiff in possession —to
collect the money for (hose greedy bond-
holders who rooked the indomitable, reck-
less, restless, pig-headed Khedive Ismail in
his old age, and induced him to play at a
species of three-card trick by which he was
cleaned out. England holds India bee a use
it pays to do so ; she keeps Eastern Africa
because it pays ; she retains Egypt because
that pays also, and because it helps her to
hold India ; and she “faces” Russia and
Fiance for exactly the same reason which
makes every dog that has a bone face the
other dog that wants to (tike the bone away.
Australia has no trade worth mentioning
with either India, Egypt or Eastern Africa,
and will have none for perhaps centuries.
They are all countries which produce almost
exactly the same materials as those pro-
duced in Australia, and unless we exchange
one lot of bananas and sugar for another
exactly similar Jot there is hardly any trade
to be done. Whether they are open to
commerce or not, whether they belong to
Britain or Russia or Gehenna, whether they
iomaili .as they are or vanish to the bottom of
the sea, makes no earthly difference to Aus-
tralia. If Britain fights for them on our
account, then it is a lost labour, a wasted and
deranged generosity, an utterly misplaced
benevolence, and a useless sacrifice, and Aus-
tralia can t be grateful over llie sacrifice, for
the ottering of the noble-hearted Britain is
just as valuable to her as three cheers are to
an alligator.
# * #
Also, there are several other points in
which Mr. Heydon is woefully incorrect.
His pathos about the dangers braved by
Old England is utterly superfluous;
England isn’t nearly so old as most of the
nations of continental Europe. Further
England whether old or otherwise—does
not “brave the worst anger” of France in
keeping the stolen principality of Egypt ;
France, as everybody knows, is watched on’
every hand by the Triple Alliance, and dare
not go to wtir with England because three
Great Powers and seven million soldiers
would straightway climb upon her'. To
brave the worst anger ” of a nation in so
tremendous a predicament is merely the
valour of a man who shakes his fist at a caged
lion. The Suez Canal is not in “British
hands ” in the sense Mr. Heydon implies—
it is in the hands of a private company of
French origin. There is no earthly reason
to suppose that France or Russia would
either “close the passage or overcharge our
tonnage ” —in any case our tonnage is over-
charged a good deal already, and, for that
matter, it is not ours but Britain’s own ton-
age, which fully accounts for the manner in
which she watches over it. Up to date,
Australia has no tonnage worth mentioning,
and when she has any Britain is as likely to
overcharge it as any other nation. The
statement that that future tonnage must
necessarily proceed through the Suez Canal
is an outrage upon geography ; it may go
cither via Cape Horn or the Cape of Good
Hope, and when the Nicaragua Canal is con-
stiuctei it will probably abandon Suez
altogether. The assertion that, next to
India, our nearest neighbour is Eastern
Africa, is another example of childlike
ignorance ; Tonquin, which Britain has
allowed to drift into French hands, is nearer
than India, and China is many hundreds
of miles closer than Africa at its nearest
point, and German New Guinea is more
adjacent than either. Therefore if Britain,
as alleged, is actuated chiefly by a desire for
Australia’s welfare she will let India and
Africa and Egypt all slide together, and hang
on with a frenzied grip to China, Tonquin,
A imam, German New Guinea, and the
French and Spanish colonies of New
Caledonia and the Carolines. Also the state-
ment that Britain asks “ not a penny of aid ”
is very much out of place considering that
that country has just traded oft’ upon us for
an exorbitant subsidy, a fleet of brown-paper
ironclads which are a by-word and a laugh-
ing stock in the realm of naval architecture.
# # #
These arc a few- only a few of the note-
worthy points in Sir. Heydon’s fearsome
outpouring. Among the others, he demands
that New' South Wales should, if necessary,
“go to some trouble and cost to impress
upon the world that she has no idea of
federating just now.” England, it seems,
doesn’t like Australia to federate. “The
pride and claim for equality from self-
asserting colonies grown to manhood’s
strength are distasteful to her,” and there-
fore it is solemnly requested that Australia
should stop growing lest the “ mother
country should led bad. Even The
Bulletin, whose opinion of Britain is in-
finitely lower than that of Mr. Heyuon,
M. L.C , declines to credit the British nation
with so utterly base and despicable a jealousy
as this, and the assertion that the progress
of another branch of her own race is “ dis-
tasteful to England is as mean a thing
as ever the meanest specimen of humanity
ever said of any people. Further, after-
asserting that Britain spends all her life in a
disinterested effort to advance Australia, the
escaped M.L.C. announces that the British
nation is “insular and narrow,” and takes
much more interest in a local baccarat
scandal than in any events which
take place outside the narrow range
of her own four seas. This statement
seems woefully inconsistent, considering
what went before, but one incongruity is of
no particular account among so many. Also,
the statement that England—again solely on
Australia s behalf—is even now “facing
powerful foes and laying heavy burdens on
her people on the Indian frontier, may be
briefly described as unmitigated folly, inas-
much as, up to the present date, the hard-
driven Emir of Afghanistan is facing the
said foes all by himself, and England isn’t
facing anybody, nor will she "be called
upon to do so till Afghanistan goes under ;
while, as for the “ burdens,” they are
laid upon the people of India, and
England takes nothing but the profits.
But all these historical and geographical
bungles are more derails ; the one ineffable
absurdity of the whole doctrine is the as-
sumption that there is, or ever was, or ever
will be a nation which does not devote itself
solely to an utterly selfish scramble for its
own aggrandisement. Mr. Heydon, M.L.C.,
is ob\ iously a human anomaly, inasmuch as he
seems to know less and to believe more than
any other man of the present age. It is a
noteworthy fact that such individuals as he
are always grown in cities. The utterly
credulous groveller is not a product of the
bush or the wilderness. It is only the man
who has sat looking for half a century at the
dead wall on the,other side of the street who
can become so hopelessly stunted in his in-
telligence and so narrow in his ideals that
he wants his country to stop growing lest it
should aggravate a small island at the other
end of the earth.
The Queensland Slave-Trade.
Rome of the Australian and South Pacific
clergy, let it he recorded to their credit, de-
nounce the Queensland (Jovernment’s proposal
to revive the infamous traffic in Polynesian
flesh-ami-blood. It is true that, of this minority
of “ white ’ parsons, a large proportion is
cynically credited with being personally and
financially interested in keeping the Kanakas
on the islands. Letting that pass, and rather
inclining to uot-too-dceply scrutinise the mis-
sionary claim that the main evangelising idea is
to utilise the islanders as labourers in “ the
Lom> s vineyard, instead of in the canefields of
the Queensland sugar-planters, there remains
still a large majority of professional Gospel-
sellers perched self-regardfnlly on the fence of
opportunism, who declare their joint and
several belief that, if “proper precautions”
against abuse are adopted, everything will bo
glad and gay for the recruited nigger, t
requires no polemical controversy, nor
it need any missionary arguments, to arrive aU
logical conclusion in this matter. A knowle'da
of human nature in its unutterable laws , c
evolution is quite sufficient for the punW
hirst, as to the islanders. What sort 0 f
ultimate development can—must—await peonies
robbed of the flower of their manhood, and ] G fr
to rely upon their senile and juvenile remnant
their ■weak and scanty womanhood’
What sort of fate awaits the departed manhood'
itsdf, kidnapped by artful strategy to a forei-m
country where much of it dies off with alarm-
ing rapidity, while the rest of it contracts
diseases which unman their victims for Hf e ?
•Nothing but appalling calamity can result to
the island-peoples, both as to the feeble
who remain and as to the vigorous who
are carried away by their white conquerors
as much conquerors as if they raided the
Polynesians by fire and sword and bore
on the balance left by rapine and open theft
then, as to the whites. All history blazons
with solemn warnings of the ultimate national
horrors consequent upon the placement of au
inferior and foreign race amongst a dominant
race which yet, being democratic, appends its
sign-manual to the ideality of human equality
Ihere can be no half-way-house between the
puiity of the race-standard and the recognition
of absolute slavery. If the dominant race be
slave-owning and slave-holding, it may be
abominable, but still logical. If it pretend to
glory in the abolition of slavery and in the
“scratch” equality of all manhood, and yet
impose labour conditions on coloured sections
of its population, it is both abominable and
ulogmah Nay, more ; it is in the very essence
of its boasted laws, in the very charter of its
■justice, hypocritical.
* * #
The newspapers tell that the Queensland
Government have received over 400 applica-
tions, many of them from young men who come
within the doleful designation “well-connected ”
for billets as Government agents in the black-
birding trade ; but that all appointments arc to
be made from the Civil Service, a boon f or
which the Service should be rebelliously un-
grateful. What possible breed of skimkhood
can applicants for this sort of dehumanising
occupation be ? Picture the life of a Queens
land Government Labour Agent—as we knew
him in the past, and as be will probably’ be to
the end of the chapter. A solitary unit on
board a small schooner, manned by man-
pouncers. He the sole representative in that
lloating family of—“ the Government.” He has
no authority whatever over the crew. If he
attempts to rigidly perform his duty to the
Government, and to be a sort of father-protector
to the Kanakas, he becomes obnoxious to the
captain, and to all whose interests are bound
up with the captain. Everybody on board but
the Government agent is there in the interests
ot t ie sugar-planter, and burns with a lust for
head-money. If the Government agent is
approachable on that side of his nature, all
goes well with his immediate comfort and sur-
roundings, and he is treated by the labour-
pirates as a decent comrade-trafficker-in-flesh.
If his integrity is proof against secret under-
standings, there is nothing on earth so easy as
to lose him accidentally in the Pacific, or on an
island. There is nothing for a just and well-
meaning Government agent to do throughout
the dreary voyage but drink, and the average
skipper’s whole energies are concentrated on
the task of making and keeping him drunk. In
nine cases out of ten, that result is achieved.
If an agent did not get drunk, and if
he returned safely to Queensland with
views of adversely reporting the vessel, there
might be found a whole crew ready to swear
that he was drunk all the time. So, as a rule
he docs get drunk. Shut out from all friendly
intercourse with those with whom he daily
mixes, he is only a man in a million who can
continually keep his mental balance and' Ids
moral integrity, and defy the combined captain
a,ml crew day after day. So the great majority
of Government agents cave in with a sigh,
accept ameliorated conditions, and drug their
consciences into liquid stupefaction. In short,
they must “ keep chummy ” with the captaim
Y hea one of these returns to some Queensland
slave-port, he and the captain go off together
on a drunken spree, it being the captain’s ashore
policy to keep paired with him, lest an irrigated
system might betray a loosened tongue. What
time the agent sits in the hotel parlour, with
bloodshot eyes and incandescent breath, and tells
hallowing talcs of Inst and blood and piracy by
land and sea, until the mind qf the listener sees
red shambles and piled-up, bleaching Golgothas.
I hen the agent tapers oft into delirium tremens,
breaks hotel mirrors, prowls along passages
clad only in a shirt and socks, and flees over
balconies to escape from imaginary demons.
Such was the Queensland Government’s South
Sea labour-agent as \vc knew him at homo.
* * *
I hat -the Kanaka proposals of cx-Wilrer-
iorce Griffith deliberately recognise slavery
as a legal institution is capable of being logi-
cally argued, and recent cablegrams inform that
this aspect of the question is being brought
before the British House of Commons by direct
resolution. Ibe following communication
relates to the same aspect of the “trade”:—
Dear Bult.ktin,—The main argument by which the
Ouikkilwraitii Government (Q.) endeavours to excuse its
Kanaka .kidnapping is that the imported n hirers are hy
a "j .eesh ictod to field-work." When any Government

so penal sed necessarily made hy statute-law actual and
confessed slaves t If any subject is hy law forbidden
to do, under any and nil circumstances, somethin"
winch is admittedly legal, right, honest, and honour-
able, then that subject il a : slave) both in fact
and ui law. Griffith's Kanakas are forbidden to
do any honest work except in the field. They
come within my definition, and are slaves both in fact and
in law. Gnu’kit 11 s Polynesian Act therefore legalises
slavery. lut slavery has been declared illegal hy British
Q" s which hold good in this-country. Therefore,
the Griffith Polynesian Act is itself illegal: or, alterna-
tively, those clauses relating to the restriction of Kanakas
to field work are illegal. If the whole Act is illegal, then
it cannot repeal the former Act which abolished Poly-
nesian labour. If only the “restriction” clauses are
illegal, then the Griffith argument about “field-
work only is ft fraud, a lie, a hypocrisy of
,]uigqn, and Kanakas may ho employed indis-
criminately hy planters, farmers, squatters, vtine-
onnuis, manners, manufacturers, merchants, store-


. „cis or private citizens, in field, run, mine, ship’
fetory* counting-house, store, garde.i, <r nursery,
&o How would the Queensland Supreme Court
i fermine this point I have railed, if it wore ingeniously
■trued before a Judge, or the Full Court? How would

PLANIN ENGLISH.
An Economical Government.
Tins is the era of rigid economy in. Queensland.
few weeks ago a labourer employed on the
Western line fell ill—his illness started on the
evening of the 25th day of the month —and two
days later he died and went, probably, to a
better land —anyhow, it would be hard to find
a worse one to go to. His wages, at the rate of
7s. a day, were paid monthly, and in the
ordinary acceptation of the term he was a
monthly servant. Therefore, his official superior,
as a matter of course, made out the voucher, on
behalf of the widow, for the ordinary month’s
pay, but on that bereaved woman applying for
the 'money she was informed that the Commis-
sioners had disallowed the amount, and would
pay only to the exact date when the deceased
quitted work. They not only docked the
mid three days of the month after his death,
but stopped the two days which he spent in
dying, and by this act of economy they saved
about 355. to the consolidated revenue, and left
a poverty-stricken widow and a bereaved family
£1 15s. poorer. Yet the deceased was as legally
a monthly servant as any pompous official in a
Government office at Brisbane, and no such
mle was ever applied to the defunct clerk in
the Lands Office or the Colonial Secretary’s
department. The Commissioners who perpe-
trated this piece of small and grovelling
economy were the same crowd who, only
a few days previously, fooled away many
pounds in providing a special train to
convey the Hon. Horace Tozer and his
female friends, relations, and hangers-on to
Tcntcrfield show because that inflated beadle
was too important to travel in an ordinary
train along with the common herd who paid his
salary. And the Parliament which tacitly
sanctioned it was the same Parliament which
recently voted huge grants to the widows of
two public servants who had drawn large
salaries for many years, and whose pay was not
stopped for the time they wasted in dying.
When a Government of the Griffith order
enters on a course of rigid economy it always
goes on one fixed principle, which is to grab
355. from some starving widow, and then grant
£lOOO to some wealthy individual who has
already more than enough, and finally to go on
a champagne burst in a special train in order to
celebrate the saving of the 355. already men-
tioned. Then it “ goes to the country ”on its
retrenchment policy, and the chances are about
even that it gets another term of office on the
strength of it.
Hanging Under False Pretences.
The question put to the jury who tried
“Williams” was, in effect, “Is this man as
sane as the average murderer who goes to the
gallows?” and the eleven citizens in the box,
many of them kindly-looking persons, decided
that Williams was comparatively a healthy
subject for judicial destruction. 'They even
went so far as to declare that no future revela-
tions, tending to suggest the physical im-
probability of this murderer being fit to sit
upon a jury, would or could change their con-
viction that he was fit to be hanged. Probably
they didn’t deny in their hearts that he miyht
be a homicidal maniac, but they were uncon-
sciously endorsing the sentiment of Crown-
Prosecutor Walsh (a sentiment fitted only
for the court and era of a Judge Jeffrey s
or a Judge Norbury), viz., that the state of
prisoner’s brain could be best settled at a post-
mortem. Judge Hodges, one must admit,
made out a strong case for the gallows, in a
weak, halting address. He pounded away
clumsily at the clumsy old law, by which a
murderer’s complete responsibility for his crime
lies in his knowledge of right from wrong, and
hastily dismissed the theory that Avilful wrong-
doing, on impulse or premeditation, may be
inspired by a diseased patch in the human
brain pulp. Henceforth Hodges cannot con-
scientiously admit kleptomania in the case
of a Avell-to-do female, avlio sneaks a bit of goods
from a draper’s counter, and hides it under her
sealskin jacket to escape detection. Also, he
Avill have to protest that a score of reprieved
lunatics, avlio, Avitbin his oavii recollection, have
established their sanity by the pains they took
to commit a cold-blooded murder Avithout leav-
ing a clue (such as Constance Kent), ought to
be hanged forthAvith. According to the law,
the sanest of all murderers must be he avlio
certifies to his knoAvledge of right from wrong
by giving himself up to a policeman imme-
diately after the fell deed is done, yet the self-
accused assassin always gels the gentlest
summing-up. The laiv,Which is an instinctive
humbug, does not religiously maintain the in-
fallibility of the crude “ right from Avrong
test. In fact, it is difficult to pin the murder
law doAvn to any fixed principle, except, per-
haps, the principle that an artful, lying homi-
cide is more guilty than his candid brother
culprit. If the devil that possesses Williams,
the slayer of his oavii children, had, in a
spirit of mischief, prompted him to con-
fess his murders before the last one was
discovered, he would probably be languish-
ing in a lunatic Avard instead of a con-
demned cell at this very minute. Now, the
fact that he didn’t so confess, and Avasn’t likely
to, has no bearing on his knowledge of right
from Avrong, yet it places him beyond all hope
of mercy from the luav. Because A illiams is
an animal, treacherous, remorseless, with a
limited capacity for cunning and a shallow
instinct of self-preservation, he is presented to
the jury as an ordinary fellow-man and judged
accordingly. By sentencing him to death on
the ground that he is a sane human being guilty
of murder, the luav insults our common
humanity, and the Judge practically argues
that he himself might be just such another beast
if he Avcre not afraid of the galloAVS* i Imt s
where the law is a humbug. It hangs Williams
under false pretences. The statutes should
have allowed Justice Hodges to ask the jury
whether they would hang the creature in the
dock as a man or an animal, and they would
have found him guilty under the animal clause.
Then the world at large could exult over the
execution of Williams, withqut trying to
believe that this particular instance of capital
punishment was justified by any higher moral
consideration than justifies the shooting of a
mad dog.
The Case of Thomas Suffielcl.
After a lengthy period of consideration the
Law of New South Wales, as embodied in Mr.
R. E. O’Connor, Minister of Justice, has con-
firmed the conviction of Thomas Suffused,
sentenced in April, 1891, to 10 years’ penal
servitude on one of the most vague and shadowy
charges of criminal assault ever heard before an
Australian court. The evidence against Suf-
field consisted of one woman’s absolutely un-
supported assertion, and nothing more. No one
saw the alleged crime committed ; there was
no collateral evidence to prove that the
prisoner had been at or near the place
where it was committed ; and on the
other hand, some half - dozen witnesses
swore positively that he was in quite a different
place at the time of the disaster. The weight
of evidence was, according to legal authority,
altogether in favour of the acquittal of the
prisoner, but nevertheless the jury convicted
him, as the usual maudlin jury nearly always
does in such cases. The average juryman is
actuated at these times by a certain muddled
feeling that the wives and daughters of the
community will be endangered if the base
ruffian in the dock is allowed to get away scot-
free, and in bis thick-headed indignation he
seldom stops to consider whether he has got hold
of the right base ruffian or the wrong one. Con-
sequently, no prisoner is ever convicted on such
utterly untenable evidence as the one who is
charged with offences of this description. It
was Suffield’s further misfortune that Judge
Windeyer, of Mount Rennie notoriety, was on
the bench at his trial, and being found guilty
he was promptly crushed with a sentence of
10 years. Then, the case being carried before
the Minister for Justice, that individual con-
firmed the Judge’s decision, and explained him-
self as follows : _____
She may or may not be mistaken in identifying the
prisoner as the man who ravished her, hut that she was
ravished by some man, and that she honestly believes that
that man was the prisoner lam convinced. In my view
of the case, therefore, the only question about which the
jury could have hesitated was the identification of the
prisoner. The sole evidence of identification was that of
the prosecutrix , without corroboration of any kind, but
clear and positive. The defence of an alibi was set up by
the prisoner, and several witnesses gave evidence in sup-
port of it. The jury, after careful consideration disbe-
lieved the defence and believed the prosecutrix. Under
these circumstances I am asked to direct an inquiry under
section 353 of the C. L. Amendment Act of 1883, upon the
facts alleged in the declarations herewith. It is altogether
beyond my province to direct a re-hearing of evidence
already given. That evidence has already been decided
upon by the jury. To review their decision upon a new
inquiry into the same evidence, would be to make a private
Ministerial inquiry a Court of Appeal from the decision of
a jury, a thing which the section never intended. It is
only where some material fact or circumstance, not before
the jury at the trial, has been brought before the Minister
that the latter is justified in directing an inquiry. His
Honor Mr. Justice Wisdkyer has, I think, defined very
clearly and accurately the scope and purport of such
an inquiry. I may say at once that in this case l
have been very favourably impressed with the prisoner’s
previous character, the general estimation in which he
had been held by those who knew him, the entire absence
of anything like concealment or shuffling at the time of
his arrest, his frank, open manner all through in dealing
with the police. Being so impressed, and knowing that
the conviction of this man rests upon the uncorroborated
evidence of one witness, I should have been glad to have
had placed before me any new fact bearing upon the
identification of the prisoner or the credibility of (he
prosecutrix upon which I could have granted an enquiry.
There are sundry damning admissions in this
document. Mr. O’Connor points out cheerfully
that the prosecutrix may have been mistaken,
which simply means that, to Mr. O’Connor’s
own knowledge, it is just a toss-up whether an
innocent man isn’t doing 10 years in gaol on an
utterly false charge. He admits that there
was only the woman’s bare assertion on one side
against the prisoner’s assertion and that of
half-a-dozen witnesses on the other. And he
acknowledges that he is “ favourably impressed
with the prisoner,” after which he winds up
with the statement that “ the verdict the jury
arrived at after a careful hearing of evidence in
open court, must stand.” Which should mean,
if words mean anything, that Minister O’Con-
nor believes, in his inmost heart, that the
prisoner is innocent, but that he must stay in
gaol as a sacrifice to certain legal precedents
unless some new facts come to light. If all the
available evidence has been already found, and
the jury has disregarded it in utter defiance of
common sense, and the principle of giving the
accused the benefit of any reasonable doubt,
then the case is beyond redress. Let us
charitably presume that Minister O’Connor is
a diplomatist, and is leaving the matter to the
public.

The New South Wales Municipalities Act
Amendment Act, fathered by Pigott, M.L.C.,
gives municipal corporations power to enforce
all arrears of rates due on any property against
the present occupant of that property, and not
only applies to all cases occurring after the
passage of the Act, but is retrospective. In
dozens of N.S. W. municipalities, there are
scores of properties that are 10, 20, 30, and
inore years in arrears of rates ; yet which have
changed hands several times. Under the en-
forcement of Pigott’s Act, this sort of history
is being continually revealed Crabb got hold
of a township allotment of land 30 years ago, and
never paid any rates. He was often sued, and
verdicts cast against him, but he took no notice
whatever and had no chattels on which dis-
traint could be made. Later, Grubb came
along and bought from Grabb for £5O. Mean-
time Pigott got his Act through. Since then,
land values having declined, Grubb wants to
sell to Grobb for £4O, but finds that, ere he
can do so, he must pay up all the arrears of
rates. Grobb knows that ha will otherwise be
liable for those arrears ami naturally insists on
this point. Grubb finds tlnn [he arrears amount
to £45. If he now sells to G'uouu he will lose £<>
in hard cash aswell as the £lO deferred loss on his
purchase. It. would actually pay Grubb better
to hand over the land gratis to anybody. All
this is a grand movement onwards towards the
coming day when land-ownership shall cease,
but it’s very hard upon Grubb, in the mean-
time. If Pigott’s Act had let the past rates-
evaders escape, and had started fair from its
own birth, the majority of the evils sought to
he cured would have been sufficiently remedied
in the case of most corporations.

Grab Bulletin, — The class which thinks that
Government ought to reward thrift, fixity, and
kindred virtues with an extra vote does not act,
as a rule, as if it were desirable that these
virtues should he recognised in the working
class. On the contrary, at every opportunity
the workers tire scattered broadcast over the
land. Here in Maoriland, after the maritime
defeat of ’OO, hundreds of families had to leave
their homes because work in the district was
denied them. These homes were the only set-off
against years of work and economy, and when
they w’ere lost the only proof of the builders’
thrift was gone. Then, lest they might become
“ fixtures ” elsewhere, the names of many of
the men were scut all over the country, so that
some of them, after travelling over 400 miles,
were told that the Masters’ Association would
not allow them to he employed. Not long ago
an employer of labour, alluding to some dis-
missed employes, said ; “ Nothing like keeping
the -s travelling. ” This policy seems much
in favour, and lias been ruthlessly enforced by
the leading companies. Let us hope that the
meditation allowed by idleness and travelling
may suggest a remedy.—C.o.o.

Murderer Deeming requests that his brain
he handed to the doctors for examination.
A similar request from murderer Colston
was refused. The official refusal was, in
The Bulletin’s opinion, worthy only of
Dahomey. It is a coincidence that both
Colston and Deeming suffered from the same
disease—which so helps to fill the lunatic
asylums and so often leaves physical traces in
the brain. Does the Victorian Government fear
the knock-down blow wdiich would he given to
capital punishment by the possible discovery of
visible disease in the convict’s brain ? Do they
fear such an occurrence as that wdiich took
place a few years ago at Deniliquin, New South
Wales, where a medical commission reported
concerning the mental condition of two con-
victs, both of whom it adjudged sane ? One
was a solitary crank who, enraged by some
children of whom he was the constant butt,
killed one with his axe. The other was a
horse-thief, who, a few days after the Grown
doctors had testified to his sanity, proceeded to
demonstrate the fallibility of human judgment.
He escaped the gaol, and drowned himself in
the Edwards River, whereupon a local doctor
sawed open his skull and found in his brain a
w-alnut-sized tumour. While we hang people,
let us dare to say why we hang them. If we
hang people whom many of our foremost
scientists suspect to he lunatics, let us say w T e
hang them merely because it is inconvenient to
keep them. The Government which, through
fear of its judgment being impeached, refuses
to allow' a dead criminal’s brain to be examined
in the interests of truth and justice and the
human race, is not much less mean than the
inhuman monster who, after assassinating
those of his own flesh, cements the carcases
under a hearthstone to shut out daylight and
baffle the officers of the law.
King Billy’s Conversion.
(FOR THE BULLETIN, BY "IRONBARK.")
King Billy was a Myall black
Of very early type,
He never used a handkerchief
Ilis royal nose to wipe ;
And he wore no sort of clothes at all
Except a short clay pipe.
lie had no sceptre and no crown,
Or anything of worth,
But he’d paint and grease himself and stalk
The land that gave him birth,
As proud as if he owned the lot
From Moreton Bay to Perth.
His palace was a gunyah built
Of boughs and bark of trees,
It smell of fish and ’possum-skins,
And dogs, and swarmed with 11er.3;
And so did Billy and his gin—
Her Highness, if you please !
He never, like some husbands, wore
His hat and boots to bed ;
But he’d lots of quaint, domestic ways
And playful tricks instead,
And he used to bang his lubra with
A “ yam-stick ” on the head.
He loved to hurj/his boomerang—
His simple joy s were few—
He mercilessly massacred
The “ old-man ” kangaroo,
And knocked from off the highest trees
The screeching cockatoo.
He snuffed the desert breezes like
The poet’s “ Arab steed ” ;j
Though rankest fumes of “ Barrett's twist ’
He much preferred, indeed.
On snakes, and almost anything - ,
IBs Majesty would feed.
He loved (a very long way off)
The battle’s noisy din,
When “ nullah nullah ” blows were struck
About some erring gin ;
But ’possums, grubs, and carpet-snakes
He simply revelled in.
He loved the forest free—the scent
Of wattle and of gum—
But, better far than all, he loved
His senses to benumb,
And fill his Royal person to
The teeth with Queensland rum !
He used to shock the mission folks ;
The missionary’s spouse
The monarch’s sense of modesty
Attempted to arouse,
But she didn’t meet with much success
As every one allows.
The Re\ erend Blobs, he merely yearned
King Billy’s soul to save ;
His wife’s supreme ambition was
To teach him to behave
With decency—wear pantaloons,
And wash, arid comb, and shave.
She thus resolved, although, of course,
She knew she had her work
Cut out, to make hi u “ titivate,
And dress, and come to kirk
An idiliee Hi" King was wont
Persistently 10 shirk.
'Twas thus that at the Mission-house
Arose a friendly strife,
Each seeking to induce the king
To change his mode of life,
A Christian feud sprung up betwixt
The parson and his wife.
They oft discussed the means of grace
For this most graceless dog :
And, touching these same ways and means,
They often came to “ log-
Gerheads,” for Blobs had faith in prayer,
llis wife had faith in “ prog !"
So, woman-like, she first attacked
The monarch’s weakest part
(She felt convinced he’d neither srul,
Nor conscience, mind, nor heart),
And she invoked the goddess cf
The culinary art.
She tempted him with promises
Of “ budgeree tuck out”—
That’s “pigeon-talk” for pigeon pie,
Boast goose, and bottled stout,
And caviare, and all that gives
An alderman the gout.
Che tempted him with choice preserves
Of apple, peach, and plum ;
And stilton cheese and tinned sardines
She even promised rum !
If he to church next Sabbath-day
(Full-dressed, of course) would conic.
Then Billy murmured “ budgerc-,”
And “ y.ohi,” meaning, “yes;”
He promised Christianity',
In future, to profess,
And, for his stomach’s sake, adopt
The vanity of dress.
Then Mrs. B. was filled with glee,
Her breast with triumph throb; l ,
She sent the king a stove-pipe hat —
The kind that’s worn by nobs—
Coat, vest, and pants that once adorned
Tlie sainted form of Blobs.
It’s true that Blobs’s pantaloons
Were thinned with fervent prayer—
He’d sat them into holes behind,
And almost everywhere—
But she patched, and darned, and thought they’d fit
The monarch to a hair.
When Billy saw the parson’s slops
He didn’t like the same,
For he knew he’d have an awful job
To fit them on bis frame,
But lie thought of “ budgeree tuck-ou!,”
And rum, and courage came.
He thrust his arms right through the 1c,3
Of Blobs’s pants at first,
Then tried to button them behind
His nock until they hurst;
Then, over all, he tried to put
The coat, and failed, and curst.
He couldn’t make things meet b( fora,
Nor fasten right behind ;
His savage limbs were ne’er before
So cabined, cribbed, conljned,
And so, the monarch’s wrath was roused,
With fury he was blind.
He wrenched his sinewy arms from out
The missionary’s bags;
Unwound the coat-tails from his legs.
And tore the lot to rags,
And hurled them in the river-bed
Among the rocks and snags !
’Twas Sunday morn—the clang of bells
Upon the air arose—
The pious Blobs walked forth to preach
(Mis mind in calm repose),
Nor dreamt that in the scrub the king
Was wrestling with his clothes.
The settlers churchward wend their way,
In prayer to bend their knees ;
The music of the mission bell
Was borne upon the breeze,
And smote upon the monarch’s car
Amid the mulga trees.
He heard and, jamming on his head
The missionary’s tile,
Stalked calmly to the mission-house
And, with a tranquil smile
Of smuggest self complacency,
Meandered down the aisle 1
The missionary’s daughter was
About to play the symph-
ony ; you’d really think they’d tried
To vaccinate the nymph,
And used some efficacious kind
Of dynamite for lymph.
She got a glimpse of Billy from
The corner of her eye ;
L’.ie hounded from the music-stool
Three feet or so on high.
And fainted in some person’s arms
Who happened to be nigh.
The panic-stricken worshippers
Took several kinds cf fits ;
Some draw their robes of righteousness
Around themselves and “gits,”
And some, in stolid wonderment,
Within their sittings sits.
Then, with a rush, through exit doors
They all commenced to cram ;
Saints jostled sinners in the aisle—
The non-elect said “ Dam
And Billy stood there all the while
As solid as a clam.
Describe, indeed, that wild stampede,
I can’t, ’twould fill a tome,
For no one ever seemed to know
How he or she got. home —
Except the king, and, supperlcss,
The bush he had to roam.
For weeks, within the mission-house,
There was domestic strife:
The missionary wept, and prayed—
The missionary’s wife
Has had enough of royalty
To last her all her life.
Of her civilising talents she
May be a little vain,
She may try them on a Cossack, or
A brigand chief from Spain,
But she'll never try to civilise
A myall black again!

•Mv Dear Sloorabinda, —
Being so accustomed to entertaining crowds of people
whom they don’t know from Adam was perhaps the
reason why the captain and officers of the Orient Co.’s
huge, new and magnificent mail steamer R.M.S. “ Ophir ”
made such an easy success of their ball on board, on 4th
mst., when they presumably had chosen their own
Usitors. The “ Ophir (named after the gold mines of
old) is a very swell lug indeed, the walls of the first
saloon being, perhaps, too lavishly adorned to please the
peaceful passenger who doesn’t want to exactly live in a
transformation scene, and even a Frenchman said to me
that there was too much gilding. Round the walls are
golden statuettes of the wise virgins, put there, I sup-
pose, with a view to moral effect on the female passenger
with gold-washed hair and fifteen tea-gowns. The Agent-
General and Mr. Devil t received their 400 guests in the
drawing-room ; dancing was carried on in the first saloon,
and indeed everywhere where two persons could find
room to twirl; and a chrysanthemum decorated supper,
<i la carte, was served with true marine hospitality in the
second saloon. *
E\erybody was. there—Captain Chum ley representing
Government House ; Paul Blouet, literature ; Signor Fob,
music; and I—well, Beauty and Tub Billetix. There
were some very graceful frocks. Madame Blouet wore
sea-green bengaline artfully mingled with fine black lace,
posies of crimson and yellow roses ; Mdlle. was a charm-
ing contrast in pale-pink silk and chiffon ; Miss Burde-
kin’s white satin duchesse trimmed with fine cream lace
and chiffon, and gold galon was very well cut; Mrs. S. A.
Joseph wore pale-blue and pink brocade combined with
blue and pink chiffon and pink roses; Miss Colland
sported the same contrast very becomingly ; Miss Throck-
morton, in scarlet trimmed black lace and jet, is too
young to carry a posy of autumn leaves ; the Misses
Dight wore white gowns and silk and crepon respectively
and successfully ; Miss Austin’s black satin and velvet
was too dowager-ish altogether ; Miss Ada Fosbery com-
bined grey and pink like an artist; Miss Manning was
there in white striped brocade with tulle waterfall and red
poppies in her hair. Lady limes and her daughter wore
black, the latter’s net skirt edged with green velvet; Mrs.
Alfred Bennett, black brocade and white silk veiled in
black lace. Posies were very much worn—so were pretty
faces, too, for the matter of that.
Max O’Rell’s rather superficial pow wow on “ Brother
Jonathan” proved good bait for “society,” and “lady-
ships ” and “ honourables ” (“ within the colony ”) were
as plentiful as hairs on a horse, “The best people, my
dear, didn’t arrive with much velocity at the “John
Bull” lecture, because that entertaining expose was
more or less of a personal matter to Potts Pointers who
bad been home and Englished, if I may invent the
brevity. But to hearken to Uncle Sam’s weaknesses was
quite another tiling, though the lecture held out one or
two critical allusions to British institutions which made
the front row petrify where it sat. When the lecturer
alluded to “German princes to whom John Bull gives
out door relief,” you could actually see Lady Rose Ray’s
spine stiffening. One thing I noticed—that Jinx O'Rell
cleverly identifies his accent with that of the nation In s
yarning about He is Scotch in “Sandy,” American in
Brother Jonathan, and if lie had criticized Egypt X
suppose he would have lectured in hieroglynhics at
advanced prices with R. S. Smythe disguised as an in-
terpreter.
In “Jack Sheppard ” (much abridged vocally) at Her
Majesty’s, on Saturday, the most noticeable things were
that the fair Billee Barlow (in a Jersey wig) never had
what we might call a Marion-Burton-fit in breeches,
and that Rosa Conroy was painfully shy of her grey satin
knickerbockers in the first act, and seemed to hunger for
one petticoat at least, though she seemed to get more
used to ’em later on. Jack Sheppard isn’t what one
would call a ward-robey spectre, but Billee cuts a lively
figure in a scarlet-and-gold coat and doeskin breeches,
and leaves a good final impression when she dismounts
(Billee is always dismounting near the footlights, by the
way) from a donkey-shay in a mulberry satin-coat and

Billee B. got one spontaneous and one mechanical
bouquet. The first fell flop from the gods.
Will it become de riyueur, I wonder, for the friends of
Ihe lady-respondent to hand her flowers on the conclusion
of the suit, as happened the other day at Brisbane, when
Mrs. Crossley was given a large bouquet of white flowers
on leaving the court with a verdict in her favour? From
"bat 1 hear of the case, Sir. C. wanted to shake off his
bonds, and Mrs. C. was accused of what the court says
she never did ; under such circumstances, if wasn’t a bad
public effect to hand her vegetables of blameless white-
ness, although it is evident that the lady’s friends were
quite su(’2j* about the matter long before the verdict, for
they must have arranged that tribute in advance! But
what a fearful thing if, after the emblems of purity had
been theatrically arranged in such a case, the court should
find their intended recipient guilty ! In that event. 1
presume the flowers would have to be surreptitiously
smuggled out of court, or gracefully handed over to the
“old man” as the innocent of the deal. But, after all, it
wouldn’t be a bad idea if the crowd in court showed how
its opinion went by handing bouquets to fair divorcees.
At bite might mean public acquittal —mixed flowers for
mixed opinions, while red would embody her social comjf,
of course. The custom would make divorce cases far more
poetical and picturesque.
This Brisbane divorce incident somehow recalls an
American story I once read. The relatives of a just-
deceased woman were consulting the undertaker about
the coffin-trimmings. “ For an unmarried woman,” said
the man of plumes and “ weepers,” “ you must have
white trimmings ; for a married woman, heliotrope ones.”
They said nothing hut went away. By and bye, the
undertaker got a note: “ Picass put on white trimmings,
with a dash of heliotrope.”
The latest sue jessfui adventuress of tho type of Sydney’s
well known “Clara ’ is said to constantly travel between
Sydney and Adelaide on the mail-boats, and to make
excellent business out of tho passengers, especially the
bald, unctuous traveller with a gold cable watch-chain
looped across and a black silk travelling cap. Of course,
she lias an infinity of aliases, hut recently she represented
herself as the daughter of a well-known captain in the
Orient Co.’s service, and attributed her untrammelled
actions to pa’s neglect. Which fable ultimately bowled
her out, for somebody whom she had impressed too
favourably with her story officiously wrote to the Orient
Co. abusing Captain Blank for letting his daughter roam
about the Pacific. The Orient Co. took it up, and Ihert
of course, tire lady had to suddenly discover that she was
a niece of Lord Jersey, or something like that. She is a
stylish, handsome woman, much-bejewelled, and gifted
with phenomenal cheek. At the saloon table one day she
sat right opposite a gentleman from whom she had stolen
a shirt, wearing a fetching blouse made out of the self
same garment.
And, apropos of adventuresses, “ Clara,” now in prison
at Melbourne, made a bigamous marriage with a young
Sydney Scotchman, who will be worth some thousands in
the sweet by-and-hy. He would he / mourning tils folly
(she represented herself as Lady Carington’s sister, and
they were married after ten days acquaintance) to this
moment had it not been for a smart Sydney lawyer who
bully-ragged the woman into a confession of a pre-
existent spouse, and got him out of the web. Max
O’Bell, who preaches that a Scotchman never made an
unequal bargain in his life, ought to prayerfully peruse
this par.
Harping about the female portion of sea-going passenger-
lists, I remember one lady (a Southern doctor’s ex-wife,
by the way, for he has divorced her) who came out on a
mail-boat and was a source of continual worry to the
gallant captain owing to her over-proof flirtations.
Indeed, so recklessly did she s’amuse that the captain
had her followed after sunset, and whenever she settled
down with a kindred (male) soul, there did a myrmidon
bang a glaring light! She was turned off the ship qt
Adelaide because, I s’pose, the electric-light gave out.

Now that ex-Mayoress BUrdekin is back from Europe
people would like to know whether the Governesses’
Home-question—which got so far as circulars in 1890—will
l>e opened up again? Mrs. Burdekin was very keen about
the Home before she went away, and she has only to
convene a meeting to set the necessary scheme in work-
ing order in no time.
It is under existing social arrangements quite out of the
question for governesses to reside at “Homes” where
domestic servants are also entertained, so boarding-
houses, either doubtful or dear, are the only resort for an
unemployed teacheress. I lately heard of one governess
who patronised a Home of the mixed sort from which she
and the new cook were engaged by the same employer oh
the same morning. As a result, Cookie meets her a day
or two later on the stairs with “Good niornin’, Miss
Blank, it’s a lot more comfortable here than at old
Mother ’s, ain’t it ?”
Apropos of nothing, it occurs to me that when the ex-
pounder of any religion escapes and tells fancy stories of
what’s found behind the altar, it would be just as well if
the heads of that faith chained up all their unctuous,
rolling-eyed disciples until he goes away and left only the
pale, thin lipped ascetics free to do the Block. What do
you think ?
If you ever happen to be socially entertaining a China-
man you should be careful, as a woman, to wear decorous
clothing, even if yon are giving him a ball. Up North—
at a time when Chinese big stores were attracting the
attention of the wholesale aristocracy it was deemed good
business for Mr. Softgoods to invite Mr. Confucius to his
house, and at first he was an attractive novelty, especially
when he turned up in full war-paint. But one evening at
a swell party, with .Madame billowing between a belt and
shoulder-straps, Mr. Hong Kong stole up to her gentiv,
and, tapping her lightly beneath her diamond necklace
with his fan, said enthusiastically; “My word ! you welly
fine woman, Missee Dash—my ciontiyman velly fon’ fine
woman allee same as you. Me thinks so too.” Mrs. S-
added another button to her frock next time John was
invited.
The outline of most male singers of note whom one goes
forth to identify has led me to the conclusion that
vocalism should he the very last resort of the dude. The
exercise of singing, as it naturally suits a woman's style
better than a man’s, also exerts a far more favourable
physical effect on my sex then it does on t’other. I
gather so much—as I said before—from outline. The
singing woman is usually rewarded with a bust that is the
envy of every flat-chested female who hails her; hut the
sterner songster—well, he achieves embonpoint, too, hut
it is below the waistband. In the masculine a big voice is
synonymous with a big waistcoat, but, of course, there
are exceptions—Foil, for instance, who looks like a bean-
pole growling its simple lay.

Big-drum Booth seems to have discovered
something sailor-like ”in the Australians, which
is more than the oldest inhabitant can manage to
detect. On occasions like Easter Monday the
young Australian may here and there betray
symptoms of a rolling walk, but, as'a rule he lias
none of the characteristics of the “gallant tar.”
In his ideas and tastes he is apt to be particularly
anti-sailor-like. Take, for instance, the aspiring
boy who wishes to clear out from the old folks at
home, and start life on his own account. In
England, lie talks of running away to sea. In
Australia lie announces his intention of “going up
country.” Booth probably based his nautical
notion of Australians upon the fact that he saw
several larrikins chewing tobacco.
In the address read to Governor .Jersey
at the opening of the Bathurst (N.S.W.) A. H.
and I. Association fehow, special stress was
laid upon the “salubrious climate and the
phenomenal prosperity of the district.” The
climate is still salubrious, but the prosperity seems
to have leaked a great deal since H.E. left, for at
Bathurst Corporation sale-yards, the other day,
the auctioneers offered stock to anyone on four
months’ bills, without interest, and yet no one
accepted. Big, healthy store bullocks were sold at
10s. (id. a head, and cows with calves at foot for
the same price ; sheep were knocked down at 9d.
ahead. The silver-lining to this particular cloud
comes in with the discomfiture of the land-
grabbing capitalists who, having foreclosed on the
unfortunate farmers and turned their splendid
farms into big sheep-walks, now find that they
have bitten off more than they can chew.
The court appointed to enquire into the
charges against N.S.W. Chief Bailway Commis-
sioner Eddy, by Schey, M. L. A., commenced its
sittings under difficulties the other day. The
mild, and affable Schey raised four-aiid-a-half
closely, printed columns of objections to begin
with. He wanted, first of all, a cast-iron guar-
antee that Eddy would take no civil action on
account of anything of a libellous character or of
any other character that he might say against
him, and Eddy promptly dumped down the
guarantee. 1 hen he wanted a similar immunity
against any criminal action, and he got that also.
Next, he wanted the other two railway commis-
sioners to give guarantees of the same kind, and
though it was pointed out that they were not in
the case at all he talked in his soft, mild voice for
nearly half an hour upon this subject. Also he
wanted a joint guarantee from the whole trio.
After that he wanted to know if the Govern-
ment would guarantee him on its own account
against any personal loss, and it was pointed
out that the Attorney-General had already done
so. Thereupon he desired to inquire if he could
be guaranteed against anything that might be
clone or left undone by any succeeding Govern-
moot. He objected to the way the charges were
diafted, and de ired to draft them over again ;he
objected to Eddy being allowed to cross-examine
him in the witness-box; ho objected to go inio
iln witness box at all; and then he objected
to everything and everybody in a large and
general way, and aft r that ho objected pro-
miscuously. Finally, being told that ho must
either go on wiljr Ifis case or collapse, he “ aban-
doned ” the first-elVnfrgfe altogether, and being told
that this was insufficient, he “withdrew” it as
well. The Commission, at the time of going to

At the Sydney Criterion, Buchanan’s;
version of Fielding’s immortal novel of “Tom
Jones,” yclept “Sophia,” is doing its best to hold
the mirror up to a nineteenth century notion of
the customs and costumes of its great-grand-
fathers. Gabriel Dante Rossetti, wo think it was
who described the gifted playwright in question
as a scrofulous Scotch poet. Buchanan retorted
that lie was not scrofulous, nor was ho Scotch, and
his “friends” completed the period by denying
that he was a poet. We think thev were correct!
“Sophia” is a splendid example of successful
degradation, by a mannikin, of a great man’s
work. Fielding’s Tom Jones was a hearty vaga-
bond with all Blifil’s vices—and some of his own—
but lacking Blifil’s hypocrisy. Buchanan's Tom
Jones is merely a jovial toper of immaculate
morals ; and all the magnificent breadth of
eighteenth-century libertinism indulged in by his
robust exemplar is wilted away in an effort to
display the manners of a past age through the
modern spectacles of cheap conventionalism. Of
course, it goes without saying that the present
Brough and Bouoicault production is splendidly
mounted and admirably acted—but why was it
not called something else? “Susan Jane ” would
have done just as well as “ Sophia,” and the
characters might even have gained in respect-
ability (that huge desideratum of Mr. Robert
Buchanan’s) if they had been attired in the garb
of the present day.
* # *
The most completely artistic and satis-,
factory work ever done, or attempted, by Mr..
Cecil Ward, is his pourtrayal of the jovial and!
robust rake whom Fielding forced upon humanity
for many years as the typical John Bull, junior : :
a good fellow, a hard drinker, a benevolent scamp,,
and an unscrupulous Lothario—a man, in short,'
after his creator’s own big, unregulated heart..
Perhaps, the palm of being next in order of;
merit must bo divided between Mr. Bond
cault’s Squire Western and Mr. Lawrence’s;
Blifil. The latter is an excellent performance, far
more plausible and even than the characterisation;
of the same part by poor Phil. Beck, whose Blifill
was certainly one of the best things he ever
attempted. Mr. Boucicault’s fox-hunting, rowdy;,
vulgar, swearing squire is a masterpiece of make-!
up, and his double-shotted adjectives are delivered!
with a vim redolent of the days of the touglii
five-bottle men whose chair of state was a saddle,,
whose sceptre was a hunting crop, and whose
deity was old port. Mr. Titheradge’s Partridge
has not mellowed with age, and the playwright
has made the character too obsequious to be pro-
bable or pleasing—hence the actor appeared some-
what to a disadvantage. Miss Patty Browne
played the part of Honor with a pert incisivencss,
a keenness of phrase and manner, which were
almost too good for this world ; but the Miss
Western of Miss Pelham, though fairly well
enacted, made one regret that Miss Romer did not
appear in her old rdle. Mr. Corlesse made a
portly and respectable Sq uire All worthy, and Mr.
Lash Gordon a sententious Square, while the
Farmer Copse and George Seagrirn of Mr. Hasker
and Mr. Mill respectively were very fairly
played. Miss Emma Temple, who succeeds Mrs.
Brough as Sophia, was graceful and adequately
ladylike; Mrs. Molyueux’s Lady Hellaston did
not seem to suit the peculiar talents of the
artiste, and Miss Spong wrestled vigorously,
and with a moderate meed of success,
with the country dialect allotted to poor,
but amorous, Molly Seagrirn. Several minor
characters were respectably filled, and Mr.
Spong’s painting of the scenery of the willow
copse was worthy ratior of a play by Fielding,
than of a play travestied from that author by a
poetaster like Buchanan.

togs of the ancient lieutenant of thieves, and
essays the ejaculation of the word “Or-der-er!”

Wild is a more or less successful copy of Mr.
Brough’s janissary chief, and many other talented
people meander cheerfully about the interstices of
the present production, and chortle joyous lays in
commemoration of Botany Bay. A wild, weird,
nerve-scaring and blush-raising terpsichorean per-
formance is a mighty feature of the show. Four
male and two female performers indulge in a
Parisian, latter-day, cholera-epoch, fin dc sieclc
can-can. Three of the male dancers are
attired as ladies ; the two ladies and the
other gentlemen are costumed as bucks of the
period. Then to the sound of riotous music
extraordinary gyrations are indulged in. The
danc-rs dressed asdandmeltes turn soul-appalling

The Sydney Garrick suffered a change on
Saturday last, when the Harry Rickards New
W llC rlish Comedy Company opened to a full house,
after a trip to the diamond fields of South Africa.
i\lr. Rickards reappears in Sydney with anew bill
a ,d new billers, and his “new” company are
absolutely up to the requirements of the Sydney
daily press. # , „
Many other Sydney shows have closed, or
are closing, “ not because they couldn’t or because
they didn't try,” but because the exigencies, &c.,
necessitate —and likewise. Max O'Hell, after a
highly successful run in an ecclesiastical vault,
beneath the Ivdeous oppression of a Wesleyan-
like organ, flits to other, and, possibly, more
appreciative shores. We are sorry to lose him,
for he began to teach us to love—no, not England,
but Maxi me O'Reilly and his superb France.
Sells’ mammoth circus also closed on Wednesday’
night with a wild Arabian whoop, and the glitter-
ing ensemble has gone the way of all horse-flesh.

still open. So also is the Ooogee marine stamping-
ground of that ilk, and various nondescript places
0 f various varietness.

The last nights of Mrs. Bernard Beere, at
Melbourne Opera House, are taking place amidst
a satisfactory display of enthusiasm by the higher-
priced public, who have laid hold of the broad
{act that Mrs. B. B, is a first-class artiste, and are
prepared to swear hardest by her least admirable
efforts. This reckless determination to bedelighted
on principle gave a general appearance of success
to “ She Stoops to Conquer,” in which the star,
as Kate Hardcastle, fought hard against natural
disadvantages and didn’t get the better of them.
The management declared it a draw, and took
plenty of gate-money, and there the matter ended.
This week's programme was arranged to display
Mrs. Beere at her full height of sft. Din., or so.
As Lena Despard and Peg Woffington, she could
conquer without stooping; to-night (Thursday)
she repeats the powerful but unconvincing Lady
Teazle performance, and to-morrow will see the
positively last Melbourne appearance of herself
and company in “Fedora.” Talking of the com-
pany, it is to be hoped that Herbert Standing has
resumed operations this week.
** * *
Gaiety burlesque follows Beere at the
Opera House, even as it danced into the vacuum
left by Bernhardt at the Princess’. On Saturday
the Musgrove combination of legs and light fan-
tastic lunacy will break out in “ Faust up to
Date,” which bears the doubtful reputation of
being a specially funny burlesque. It will be
mounted regardless of the prevailing depression,
and with an eye to another Gaiety boom. Indeed,
the spec, seems full of promise. Several of the
new damsels have already produced a paralysing
effect on the “ Block,” and a young crop of alleged
“silver kings” is said to be waiting for the
sickle.
** * *
The other “Faust” will disappear from
the Alexandra forthwith, so as to avoid any con-
fusion of ideas concerning the ancient and up-to-
date devils. Dampier has decided to retire in
favour of the more festive Beelzebub, for fear that
be might be called upon for a song in the middle
of the garden scene, or have his feelings further
harrowed by the spectacle of daughter Lily
dancing a breakdown at the earnest request of the
gallery. “Faust” chases his love-sick virgin
round the shrubbery for the last time this (Thurs-
day) evening. To-morrow is a Shakespearian
Friday, and the same young couple—Miss Lily
D. and Alfred Rolfe —will breathe their vows
anew as Romeo and Juliet. The story told by
Divine William indicates that Margaret and her
lover didn’t lose much by omitting to get married.
Anyway the confiding little fraulein went up to
Heaven on a cloud for certain, whereas Juliet’s
post-mortem movements are a matter for grave
doubt. On Saturday, the Aleck reverts to red-
hot local melodrama, a four-act shocker entitled,
“Dead Beat; or The World Against Him,”
having been run-up for the occasion.
** * *
Widow O’Brien, of immortal memory, has
returned to the bosom of her family at the Royal.
The actual departure of J. F. Sheridan is nigh at
hand, therefore all who haven’t seen “ Fun on the
Bristol ” more than ten times are exhorted not to
miss the few remaining opportunities for bidding
Bridget good-bye. Slade Murray is a feature in
the variety portion of the show, and the glories of
the new ditty “ Ta-ra-ra-boom-der-ay ” are suc-
cessfully unfolded by J.F.S. and Gracie White-
ford.
** * *
“ Uncles and Aunts,” which has succeeded
“ The Solicitor ” at Melbourne Bijou, reminds one
of the delirium tremens that comes close upon the
heels of a raging drunk. The central figure is
neither an uncle nor an aunt, but a bottle-nosed
nightmare named Zedekiah, who crawls around
tables on his hands and knees, and petitions
womankind in general to call him “ Kiali.” He
resembles nothing else in Heaven or earth, and
even in the awful capacity of a wandering outrage
he is inconsistent and not to be relied upon. It
would be hard to discover what family of human
atrocities this Zedekiah Aspen belongs to, but he
tends to drive dull care away, which is about all
that anybody expects of him. Mr. Oannam, who
impersonates the apparition aforesaid, is sup-
ported by much the same company that pushed
“Uncles and Aunts” along in Sydney. With
Messrs. Brough and Fabert as uncles, Miss Romer
and Annie Taylor as aunts, Mrs. Brough and
Brenda Gibson as cousins, and Cates and Chiches-
ter as the inevitable mashers in attendance it goes
without saying that the fun never flags, although
we say it all the same.
** * *
“ La Cigale,” revived at the Princess s on
Saturday, was exactly as before with the exception
of two new faces in the stupid low comedy parts,
originally played by Elton and Lauri. The task
of following Elton in a Dutch character comes
very easy to J. 0. Williamson, whose unforced
style is a startling contrast to Elton’s painful
attempts at raising laughs from stony ground.
Elton, with his Cockney dialect, could make
nothing out of Vanderkoopen, and Williamson is
equally unable to find jokes that are not there.
But he makes a comprehensible Dutchman of the
famous John Stofel pattern, and adds touches of
nature to the opera without ever becoming weari-
some. Leumane is quite harmless as the other
man, and for the rest they are all worthy of the
nice things generally said about them. Mane
Halton will appear no more in Melbourne after
Friday, they say. Anyway, she is to take a fare-
well benefit on that occasion in the character that
shows her at her best.
« *. « » , , ,
On Saturday the pick of Simonsoti s late
opera company start a short trill at the Princess s.
The arrangement is to sing them four nights a
week in change with an English opera company,
or anything that comes handy, and if there isn’t a
crowded house to hear Cuttioa in ‘‘Lucia,’" why,
the old belief in Melbourne’s musical taste had
better curl itself up and expire. Miss Colborne
Baber, as Maritana, was to open the Anglo-Saxon
portion of the season, but there is now reason to
suspect that Jennie Lee, in “Jo,"’ will meander
like a murky rivulet through the meadow of
Italian harmony. In which case people will have
to take care lest they get Cuttica when they want
the historic crossing-sweep, and vice-versa. The
announcement that a “complete resume, giving
full details of the plot of each opera, will be dis-
tributed gratuitously ” will suit the ordinary low,
vu’gar person who has a mania for wanting to
know what it all means.

The most popular recitation at Christchurch (M.L.)
smoke-concerts (and a great many other places) is fb e
Billiard-Marker’s Story,” by Edmund Fisher, and which
was first published in the ’9O Christmas number of Tub

Editor Bulletin. Sir,—l see you mention that “Ta ra-
ra-boom-de-ay ”“is an old Swiss volkslied.” That may
be so, but if the subject is not already threadbare I can
o-ive you a few explanations. In June, ’86,1 was rowing
down the Meuse with some friends. At Mpesyck, on the
Bebnati-Dutch frontier, they were building a bridge and
singing the while. The piles were being driven by the
primitive “ monkey ” or “ mouton,” which was raised by
means of about 30 lines rove over blocks with a man the
otherend. The “ Boom’’-for “Ta-ra-ra,” &c., vyastheir
son „-—was short and dull, corresponding to the tall of the
monkey on the pile: the “de-ay” corresponded to the
hauling in of the slack and rattle of the blocks, &c.j the
“ta-ra-ra” corresponded to the three “holts” whilst
hauling up the mass of iron. 1 distinctly remember hear-
in v the refrain towards sunset as we were coming round
the curve; and, having already heard it, guessed what it
was. The men were chiefly Flemings, and told me in the
evening at the “Kstaminet” that, as long as they and
their fathers could recollect, the song had been in use—
and there is a good deal of pile-driving in those parts.
The proper time, therefore, for the air would be (o
imitate Ihe action of the men.—Yours truly, R.S. Hotel
Metropdle, Sydney, 6th May, ’92.

Analytically considered, “Jacques Bonhomme” is Max
O’Rell’s worst lecture. No man is fully alive either to his
own faults or those of his country. It is also his least
humorous ; no man cares to be funny at his own expense.
Compared with the other discourses, it is a piece of special
pleading.
Another head is being moulded for the figure of
Williams at Melbourne Waxworks. The original face
was not an altogether truthful image, but it had to be
fixed up in a hurry, because a hungry public couldn t
wait for a striking resemblance. A nice, clean-looking
young man, with a wild eye and a silky moustache, and a
liberal supply of cement, was good enough to go on with
for a time. The new Williams is intended to attract the
ladies who have engraved his features on their hearts,
and dispel their gloomy conviction that they will never
look upon his like again. Every wrinkle in the new
Williams is a profound study from life. “ You 11 he
astonished when you see him,” said Phil Stuart to the
writer, and he might well say that. Murderers in wax
are the most astonishing circumstances under the sun.
A Melbourne music-hall has started a new plan of
making the shekels. A man goes round the suburbs, and
gives every child he meets a ticket for a matinee, to be
held on the following Saturday. The ticket admits the
child to the front seats, if accompanied by an adult the
price of an adult’s ticket being a shilling. Of course, the
children badger their parents to take them, and so a very
fair crowd turns up at the matinee.
George Coppin has discarded the one-horse buggy,
driven by his daughter, for a beauteous barouche drawn
by a bay and a grey, and driven by a solemn charioteer in
an emerald ulster, in the shadow of which George lolls
luxuriously alone and beams upon the Block. The
smaller shareholders of the Royal Co. whisper that the
second horse is a property charger, and that the coach-
man is capable of playing Conn at a moment’s notice.

Allan Morris, as the Mikado, puts all his Australian pre-
decessors into the shade. His grimaces and business are
ridiculously funny, and he sings the music in a style that
the others' couldn’t touch. The Melbourne people, who
had already reckoned Morris up as a dry-hash performer
after seeing him in two feeble background characters,
were amazed at the way in which he let himself out in the
G. and S. opera.

Alice Leamar, formerly of the “Sisters” Leamar, comes
with the new Gaiety burlesque crowd. She will set many
Cockney Australians thinking of old times, when she and
Nellie L. (who wasn’t her sister off the stage) used to sing
“ Just escaped from the Harm” at four music-halls every
niirht. “Harem” was pronounced so as to rhyme with
“ scarum,” and the song was a delirious success. Nellie
dissolved the sisterhood by marrying a younger offspring
of an earl, and now it seems the scion of nobility has
successfully “ sacked ” little Nell for misconduct with one
Yates, “a gentleman of fortune.”

The Waterloo carries the Paris on its shoulders. The
second was an obvious mistake, but the Waterloo is like
Rainer’s American War Panorama, perennial, and it was
the same in London, where they would take to Waterloo
anytime, but did not care for others.
A Frenchman, named Dourof, is holding a unique rat-
exhibition in Paris. He has constructed a miniature
circular railway with a train, consisting of a locomotive,
three carriages, and a goods van. At a given signal from
the station-master, a rat, the engined river, mounts the
locomotive, the guard enters his van, and between twenty
and thirty passengers, carrying luggage, take their places
in the first, second, and third class carriages. They are
all rats, and the station-master and the worker of the
signals also are rodents. A whistle is sounded, and off
goes the train, the motive power being supplied by clock-
work. M. Dourof describes the rat as the most impres-
sionable of all animals, with a sensitively nervous
temperament, fragile in constitution, prone to melan-
choly, and apt to commit suicide when ill or “out of
sorts.” Out of 230 animals he has in training, as many as
thirty will be on the sick list at one time.
When Sampson, “ the strong man,” was sentenced, at
the London Criminal Court, to 12 months gaol for steal-
ing his mistress’ jewellery, he “ uttered a loud groan and
fainted in the box,” and the mass of discomfited beef was
removed, with difficulty, by half-a-dozen warders and
policemen. It is one of the of the big
man to collapse like this. The'gladiator is generally a
person with scanty hair, small eyes, an utterly expression-
less face, and a neck like a tree—and he looks as if lie w as
equal, in his own dull, ox-like way, to any fate, yet when-
ever he meets a difficulty' that can’t be bashed with his
gigantic fists, he falls down and grovels in abject
wretchedness. -

Lawrence Irving’s first appearance in London cannot lie
called a success, says The Bulletin correspondent. He
has undoubtedly a good vein of comedy in him, but from

great impression.
Antoinette Sterling, the famous vocalist, has just joined
the Society of Friends. She was received in the com-
munity with much ceremony and prayer, and she declared
that until then she never knew what true religion is.
Jacques, the fasting-man, has been sued in London for
medical fees. He pleaded inability to pay, but as he
recently published a book in which he stated that he made
over £3 a week by his fasting performances the Judge
who tried the case humorously pointed out that a man
who could live on nothing and earn £3 a week should be
able to pay his doctor.

They were playing “La Sonnambula” recently a*
Turin, when the leading danseuse suddenly advanced to
the footlights and'fell head first into the orchestra, dead.
We always hear of things like these, somehow, hut the
lady who falls dead in a, prayer-meeting passes almost
unnoticed.
“Ma Femme,” a four-act farce which failed badly at
Paris a little while ago, is said to have possessed only one
interesting feature. At a certain stage in the proceedings
the leading lady took off her dress before the footlights,
and appeared ‘‘amid tremendous applause ” m her stays
and petticoats. This isn’t an exclusively French proceed-
ing, by (lie way. A few years ago a male performer took
off’his whole suit of clothes on the stage of a Sydney
theatre, and the applause was deafening. Also he was
recalled, evidently in the hope that he would take off his
underclothing too.

One of the weirdest sights to be seen in
Sydney is the hundred-guinea-prize-poet Anarchist
Andrews, who sometimes holds forth in the
Domain. To see this remarkable man is to get a
glimpse of another world.
Prince George of Wales has a much
smaller head than his father; and a London paper
suggests the probability of the crown being
reduced in size when it is passed along to the son
of the present heir-apparent.
Artist Leitch (not Leech) taught Queen
Victoria drawing, and one day, the pencil dropped
on the floor. Both stooped, and knocked their
heads. Such is the awful record of Frith, in his
new biography of John Leech.

started in Adelaide years ago as a book-fiend, and
subsequently went in for importing, collecting,
auctioneering. He is now plain “ agent.”

because of the certain pension. Though the bar
may bo more immediately profitable, it only lasts
with the physique.

Judge Hodges has a hairless, angelic cast
of countenance—a sweet face, in fact—and he
talks like a curate at a tea-fight. His general
tone during the hearing of the big murder case
was that of a gentleman who had communed with
Ins soul and discovered a subtle connection
between the gallows and the glory of God.
