Marshall Lyle, Deeming’s lawyer, is an
ardent Home Ruler and Land Nationalisation
advocate, and has lectured on both these subjects
in Melbourne. He was partner during the land-
boom. in an estate-agency business, and subse-
quently played second fiddle in a building society.
Being an Irishman he naturally wants to get into
Parliament.
Christie Murray, writes a London corres-
pondent, has come honourably out of his financial
troubles. His bankruptcy was brought about by
the pressure of a solicitor, who had bought a £lO
debt of Christie’s for £5, and by means of law-
costs brought the sum up to £5O, which Chri-tie
refused to pay. This matter has now been settled,
and Murray, having passed his examination, has
now paid liis creditors in full. He has been able
to do this out of the sale of his new novel, shortly
to be issued.
It is foreshadowed in an inspired news-
paper paragraph that a good deal of Parkes’ forth-
coming book will consist of letters he has received
—or let us say, extracted —from leading men.
The letters that L’arkos lias got are the pride and
joy of his life. Every visitor to whom ho shows
them takes the compliment as a personal one until
he becomes aware that the old man has shown
them to every other visitor.
The head of the great final of Bolckow*
Vaughan & Co., L united, the largest iron people
in England, has just “gone bung.” This gentle-
man's nephew, young Vaughan, some years ago
inherited more than half a million from his father,
which lie knocked down in less than ten years.
His house was a perfect curiosity. The furniture
of his bedroom (he was a bachelor) cost something
like ,£lO,OOO, and the spittoons in the billiard-
room cost £'M) each, He was not a gambler—he
simply lot his money pass through his hands like
water.
Mr. Wentworth Reeve, who is such a
devoted supporter of English racing under Hunt
Club rules, is a grandson of tbe great W. C.
Wentworth. His father formerly owned the land
on which the Pitt-street frontage of Sydney Post
Office stands. Another grandson of Mr. Went-
worth's, Mr. D’Arcy Reeve, is a well-known figure
at London National Liberal Club. He is a
right-down Radical, and gives <£loo a year in
prizes for essays on Radical subjects by members
of tbe Eleusis Club at Chelsea. It is enough to
make his grandfather squirm in his grave.

Jvilda,” and “having two fine bridges over the
Parramatta.”
Herbert Palmer’s Imperial Review (Mel-
bourne) which has outlived all the weakly maga-
zines that have been invented in Australia, gets
most flattering notices in London. Yet, such is
the determined boycott on the part of Australian
newspapers against any literary effort that
breathes with a purely Australian breath that,
beyond the circle of the Melbourne journalists’
club, and the odd liere-aiid-there readers of Aus-
tralian magazine literature, the Imperial Review
is practically unknown.

Tho late Frederic Bjgge, the news of
whose death in England has been recently received
was a pioneer squatter of Moreton Bay. The old
firm was Frederic and Francis Bigge, after whom
“ Bigge’s Camp” (rechristened by the altiloquent
Governor Bowen “ Grandchester ”) was named.
F. and F. Bigge also at one time owned Mount
Brisbane station, famous for the Westminster and
1 ouclistone breed of horses. “Bigge's Folly” was a
monumental wool store which the same adventurers
built at Cleveland, Q., in the earlv fifties when it
was fondly supposed by a few that Cleveland was
to be the boss seaport of the then “North.”
Double Destiny.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There were few women in the place.
Those present were in high demand; Pairing-ofi
did not take long; In the absence of a clergyman
mutual agreement settled the thing. So one fin,.*
comely woman and a hardy miner became mates’
and the lady at once left the calico pub., and tool!
refuge in her lord’s tent.
Some time afterwards he was engaged cutting a
race about twelve miles from tbe township. As th<>
country was very rough bo camped at bis work
leaving his spouse behind him. But he went home
regularly each Saturday evening. His woman to
beguile the weary hours, was very familiar with
another man. One Saturday morning, when a
child which had been born to her and the minor
was three weeks old, she wrapped it in its father's
great-coat, put it on the bed, and fled with No 9
* * #
Condensed milk saved the infant’s life.
Though its father regarded it with suspicions eve
lie nevertheless placed it under the care of a
neighbouring family, and it prospered.
* * •
Twenty-three years slipped away. The
child was now a strapping man. Few remembered
the story of bis mother’s flight. The young fellow
was at work in his claim one afternoon when a
huge boulder fell from the face, and, striking the
pick, drove it into his chest. He died after two
hours of horrible agony. This suffering was
probably the expiation for his mother’s crime as
per non-blood-poisoning Scriptural records.
* • #
Stancdng back from the candle-light, in
the hotel parlour, I recognised the lad’s mother
The tears were falling fast. The flickering gleams
played strangely on that haggard face. It needed
no close scrutiny to show the furrows on her brow.
Her bent and trembling frame was the sport of
her unstrung nerves. Shame and hesitancy sat
on her features. In her eyes was the baleful light
of a wild despair. Advancing slowly towards the
corpse, she turned to the diggers around, and in a
broken voice, told of her desertion of the baby
I hen she fell upon the corpse of her son, and went
afresh. 1
Well has Schopenhauer said that “to live” is
sufficient punishment for the greatest crime.

Dear Bulletin, — Sydney Mail reports
the baptism, in England, of a child born headless
that lived for sixteen hours. This is certainly a
tremendous swallow even for a godly print like
the Mail. Did the thing breathe? Tf so how’
Did it live without breathing? Again,’ how?
\Vas the brain in the left knee, and the mouth in
a big toe? Was its mental-power generally-
reckoned to equal that of a freetrade editor? And :
so on. But, assuming the journalist has not mis/
taken a comic paper for the transactions of a„,
biological society, or merely tried to stem the ebb-’,
mg tide of subscribers with an extra whopper, let*
us take the Gospel side of the business. Are the..
reverend men now fighting “wicked,” as* the'!
boxers say ? Where do they propose to dpw a
line If a birth appears short, not only of, head
but legs and arms, will it be “the makings.”of an ’
immortal spirit? If only two ounces of stomach or -
three of rear ward ness, represent the nsuall infant
wibe eligible by sacred sprinkling to angelic joys? <
Ur it the ‘ babe ” consists simply of two grains of
tissue ” will the Christian medium man under-
take to enroll it among the seraphic choirs ? Had |
the Mail’s horror been possessed of a head only
1 would point out to the churches that its chance .
of cnerubism would have been greater. For we.
know that celestial infants are deprived of trunks ■
the cause whereof, after long study, I have found)
to be that they may not disturb the eternal!
hymnists with strains founded on “ tuinmvache.’”
iossibly also to prevent the unseemliness of the*
severer elders of the glorified treating the errors,
of childhood in the usual manner. Desperate are,
the devices of the desperate. Their altars crum-
bling beneath their feet, their auditors listening-
rather than leave them breadless, the clergy ini
some instances struggle to retain their powers by-
expedients worthy of Mumbo Jumbo. Oh, LordV,
hasten the end !—Yours truly, Ambrose Biekch:
Jun. Harden, N.S.W.

The Sydney Trades and Labour Council
refused to join the Socialists in celebrating
Labour Day. A silly and unworthy jealousy
prompted the small majority to decline to take
part in what is fast becoming a world-wide move-
ment. However, Burgess, Petrie, Wilson and
others of the Council, by their presence at the
Sunday’s demonstration, disclaimed any respon-
sibility for the Council’s action.

It is about time that Moniington (L. L.
Smith’s pocket borough) took a step in a Down-
ward direction. Downward is always classified as
a Conservative, and L. L. Smith goes on the
Liberal tick.et, but the former can’t be more
offensively loyal and Hinglish than L.L., and he
has undoubtedly worked harder in a more modest
sort of way. The non-resident vote pulls the
mercurial doctor through. At Beaconslield,
Erankston and Cranboiirne, whither ground-
holders can run down comfortably from town, ho
polls more than the majority which puts him in,
whilst Moniington proper is 3 to 1 in favour o
Downward. In fact, Moniington is the one special
part of the electorate which Smith isn't member
for.
London financiers (says a cablegram) resent
the Maoriland Premier’s recent utterance that hi*
Government seeks to wean that country horn a
“ servile dependence on the London money-
market.’ Just so. The “beastly kawlmies are
ungrateful when they don’t fall ou their niuiow-
bones in gratitude to the London pawnbrokers for
favours bestowed at a better percentage than the
three-balls-men could secure anywhere else on as
good a security; and they are thankless and
unnatural wretches if they propose to become in-
dependent and self-supporting, and thus rob J.
Bull of his opportunities of usury. In short, Aus-
tralia is expected to borrow only when it suits
England', and to abstain from borrowing only
when England hasn’t got it to lend. “Vot,.' 1 D
you don’t vant to pawn your vest, then you vo got
to buy a vateh ! ”
A N.S,W. ■country resident I)ought an
excursion railway ticket recently for Sydney and
return. Business compelling him to overstay m
town the period for which his ticket was available,
ho sought the good offices of the member foi lhis
district, said member happening to be a Minister
of the Crown. The Minister asked the kail way
Commissioners to extend the period of his con-
stituent’s ticket, and felt hurt when his request
was officially refused. A few days later, it came
1o his cognisance that the Railway Department
had for three years been occupying, free of charge,
a portion of his official quarters, so hepiomptl>
got even by notifying the Commissioners to vacate
the said premises as soon as convenient, at the
same time forwarding them an account fur <£-■ lto
for back rent. The glorious system of govern-
ment by personal magnetism feels itself once more
vindicated. ___

Do women dress for each other, or for the
opposite sex ? A clever young woman has decided
that it is for women that they dress. If for men>
society would be a gigantic corps de ballet.
A Western N.S.W. woman undertook last
week to “break the news gently ” to the Widow of
a poor man killed on the railway-line, ohe
knocked at the door: “Your husbands been
killed by a train. Cheer up, mum ;he was a fine
man, but there’s as good fish in the sea as ever yet
was ketched.”
A Queensland bullock-driver spread ail
inch-thick layer of mustard over his bare chest,
wrapped some flannel around the plaster, and slept
peacefully till daybreak. Then he arose and
rinsed off the mustard and his skin, lie troubled
no faith:r. Inflammation set in, followed by
mortification and death.
It is suggested in Melbourne Standard
that Deeming be skinned after death and that
several volumes of the trial reports bo bound in
his hide, for presentation to Marshall Lyle, Miss
llounsevell, and the arresting detectives. Lut
why stop so short? Let Deemmg’s skull bo
mounted in silver, and presented to Gov. Hope-
toun as a christening-bowl. His shinbones would
make decent clubs for the local policemen who
dug up the corpse, and one of hi* thighbones, pro-
perly mounted, might officiate with dignity in
place of Victoria’s lost, stolen, or strayed parlia-
mentary mace. The remaining osseous fragments
should be divided up amongst the Melbourne
editors.
r I lie Victorian experiment of village settle-
ments as one narrow sluice-valve of opportunity
fur surplus labour, and which may be regarded
as fairly on its trial, will be watched with
interest. The venture, so far, is a private Scheme
organised mainly by Parson Horace Tucker.
Land has been secured by an association at
Jindivick, on the Tarago River, Vic., and the
first batch of 10 families, consisting of 47 persons,
was despatched there a few days ago. The plan
proposed is to locate each settlement in a district
where the settlers can be employed on wages part
of the year, and to allot to each family a 10-acre
patch on which earnings may be supplemented by
husbandry. Jindivick is in the midst of a fertile
area largely devoted to fruit-growing. The
settlers are to be encouraged to become freeholders
of their lots, by payments extended over 5 years,
with the small deposit of =£l as a required
preliminary. A collateral development with the
idea of village settlements is that the Victorian
Government has promised to bring in a Homestead
Settlements Bill, embracing a similar scheme of
combining wage-labor with independent tillage,
the area of the holdings to range from 5 to 20
acres.
Dear Bulletin, Zola, in “La Bete
Hnmaine,” has a character, one Lantier, an
engine-driver, who in many respects resembles
Deeming. Lantier is the helpless victim of an
inherited murderous instinct which has taken an
uncommon form. Ho luxs never felt the repio
ductive instinct aroused without its being suc-
ceeded by an overmastering desire to take the
life of the woman who has been the conscious or
unconscious cause of his emotion. He vaguely
ruao-ines that the origin of this terrible perversion
is a desire to revenge bimself upon women for
some great wrong inflicted upon him or his in the
remote and forgotten past. Lantier is represented
as a man of refinement and attractive appearance,
but is a member of a family through which runs a
nervous disease, which drives his brother to
suicide and oilier members of the family to various
special forms of insanity. On everything else he
is perfectly sane and even very intelligent ami
humane. When we compare him with that strange
monster Deeming, it is evident that in Lantier
Zo'a has shown us not an individual, but a type,
—Yours &c., S.A.R.

Review publishes an article on the in-
• indebtedness of the Australian colonics.
The paper says that the entire fabric of
Australian civilisation is built upon debt
and that another “ credit storm” in London
will poscibly drive the colonies into bank-
ruptcy.
The writer concludes by urging the
'felonies lo only resort to a minimum of
borrowing, a«d points out that the present -
is only the beginning of bad times.

The N.S.W. coffin-fiend, who goes about
ordering “ caskets ” for live people—acquaintances
■of his—and paying for them with valueless cheques
out of which he receives change, has been arrested
at last. To the coffin-joke he, it is alleged, has
also added the horse-stealing trade.

» *
Williams making his ling speech in the
'dock with staring, anxious eyes turned towards the
jury, was the unmistakoable original of the Rock-
hampton photo, published in The Bulletin a
few weeks ago. The man’s full or three-quarter
face is weak and sometimes young-looking,
whereas Ins profile is full of strange character, and
the wrinkles round his eyes and the furrows in his
receding brow might belong to a keen old fellow
■of GO. He reddens as lie laughs, and from any
point of view appears to most advantage when his
fancy is genuinely tickled, as it was by the cough
<of Walsh. Q.C., during the Judge’s summing up.
Walsh, with a “voice like the squeak of a con-
sumptive shrimp” and a cough to match, had been
■a fund of amusement to him all the while. It is
Williams’ grin that gives the wild animal away.
He hasn’t educated himself up to cool contempt
and a refined sneer. His insolence is vulgar,
aggressive and villainous. Addressing the jury
be occasionally struck a soft spot in the heart of
the court, although, for a Lancashire man, he puts
very little pathos into the familiar West of
England tune. But when lie sassed the Judge
and stiffened his straight upper-lip under the final
ordeal, the poor devil excited no compassion. It
is one thing to meet trouble like a man, and
another thing to meet it like a nasty little beast.
« • •
Sheriff Anderson gave a certain tone to
the Williams trial, especially after dusk. In his
official dress, and behind the judicial candles, he
presented the appearance of an elderly, languid
swell who had been called away from a dinner-
party to sit on the Bench and play the part of
n distinguished patron. The entertainment .bored
bim dreadfully though. Considering that hp was
likely to have to demand the body of Williams
(from somebody in a few days time, the Sheriff
■seemed singularly indifferent on the subject. One
•expected him to take a keen personal interest in
Williams’ body, instead of which he gave every-
body the impression that the sudden
•ance of the plumber by spontaneous combustion
wouldn’t matter a rap to tlie Sheriff. Sheriffs are
supposed to be fat, unwieldy persons who glare at
itho prisoner in the dock and wag their heads
approvingly whenever the prosecution gives him
beans. Anderson doesn’t increase the horror of
the scene in this way. He would prefer, appar-
ently, to propose the Judge’s health in the usual
meat speech, and then clear off in a cab.
# # #
The witnesses engaged in the Deeming
trial, when ordered out of court, were herded to-
gether in another court close by until wanted.
The press-room was opposite this cave of Adn'lam,
and some of the witnesses preferred it to the larger
and colder apartment. Hence, it was no uncom-
mon thing on any of the four days fur the scribes
on returning to their sanctum after an adjourn-
ment to find Katie Rounsevell and her sister toast-
ing their little toes at the press-fire and chatting
pleasantly with two constables over bread and
.cheese and bottled boor.
# * #
Deakin made mincemeat of Dr. Shields
when he came to cross-examine that Government
functionary on the proofs of Williams’- sanity
Shields had found Colston to be sane because be
approved of the Ten Commandments and generally
spoke the truth. For exactly opposite reasons ho
considered “this man” equally responsible for
his actions. “This man” lied like a tombstone
and didn’t care for the Commandments at all.
The prison doctor’s tost was reversible, it appeared,
and if one side of it didn’t meet the prisoner’s
case the other one was bound to do the trick.

The “ Windsor Murder ” group at Mel-
bourne Waxworks is fixed up in the little ante-
room formerly set apart for Kreitmayer’s master-
piece, “ The Widow’s Mite.” A gruesome
suspicion that the two chief characters in the
Biblical tableau were melted down into Williams
and the corpse has got abroad, but there is no
truth in it. The Widow, it appears, has been
taken on tour by Kreitmayer, together with G. N.
'Baylor, Larkins, Cleary, and a duplicate of the
Hearthstone Horror aforesaid. What a beautiful
moral is taught by that travelling waxworks !
Better for the Widow to drop her little thrup’ny
in the bag and lay up treasure in heaven than to
deposit it in a land-boom bank, or a South Mel-
bourne building-society.

# # *
The other morning in a N.S.W. Govern-
ment Department a subordinate knocked at the
door of his chief, and receiving no answer
attempted to enter. The door yielded but an
inch or two, and from the character of the resist-
ance on the other side, the horrified subordinate
concluded that his boss was in a fit.' Then five
men threw themselves upon the door and forced it
open. The senseless form of the boss was upon
the floor, his knees tucked up to his chin, his hat
upon his brow. Then, remembering how he
slumbered right through the uproar of last Mel-
bourne Cup they left him alone in his glorious
snooze. Some few months back this same Civil
servant went to office on Sunday morning. He
I)ad gone to bed at eight on Friday evening and
slept until nine on the Sabbath morn, when he
hurriedly dressed, and, without any breakGs l -,
dashed off to work.
« * *
A couple of years ago, a IM (N.S.W.)
selector was sued by his lady-love for maintenance.
There was overwhelming evidence to prove that
the charmer had many admirers, so she was cast
out on a pitiless work 1 , with li >r fatherless child.
Subsequently, what Sara Bernhardt calls another
“little accident” befell her, and this time she
“summonsed ” a second man successfully. A few
weeks after, the first old love appeared with her
before the local priest. “ You wish to be married ?”
inquired the priest. “Yes. There is nothing so
sweet as our first love, and I’ve always been
jealous of her; somehow she knows how to keep
me jealous. I can’t rest aisy until I marry her.’
The priest joined them and blessed them. Truly,
love is a romantic passion.

The Womanhood Suffrage Bill has just
been narrowly defeated in the Commons. When
woman does got the suffrage the much-married
woman will want more votes thin the single
woman, because, iu the children, she has “a stake
in the country.” Si the Dear Children Suffrage
Billjwill come along, and after this Baby must
have a vote, bless its heart. Consequently, the
only man of importance will bo the one who has
fourteen cradles going at once, and when he
becomes Premier his wife will want the Head of
the Opposition for the baby to play with, and the
gaping infant will probably end up Gy putting that
dignitary’s nose in its mouth and burning its
tongue.
The idea of a visit of a team of Australian
lawn-tennis players is laughed at in London. It
is pointed out that fourth-class players who go to
“ the colonies ” can easily hold their own with the
best local players.
# # #
Th o- Flemington and Caulfield magnates
have at last decided to suppress those females
afflicted to hanging around the gates on race days
and asking members of the club to pass them
through. A member who ventured to tie Ids
ticket to his coat button and walk up to the gates
without his full complement of ladies (2) in
attendance, was always a fair mark for the
charmers who wanted to get into the show
“on the never.” They seemed to work in
unison, and probably drew lots for the
order in which they should waddle, or glide, up
to the next member with a vacancy to spare, and
cadge his company through the barriers. As a
rule, the attacked party gave way, for want of
pluck to say “no,” and the result, for years past,
has been a steady increase, in the army of
courtesans and fat women who “ were to meet a
gentleman who must have passed through without
seeing them.” Henceforth a policeman or two
will be engaged to look after these fraudulent
dead-heads, and if they- are seen swooping down
upon stray members the law will run them in for
soliciting in a public place, or inciting an innocent
stringer to commit a felony.
# * *
The gravedigger at Penshurst (Vic.) got
badly “left ” the-other day. It was the custom of
this humorist to get even with his enemies by
digging their graves when they fell sick, and
publicly announcing that he had done so. He
performed this delightful operation for a well-
known resident a fortnight ago, and put in the
time waiting for his victim, who was very ill, by
asking the latter's friends when he might expect
the corpse? Sad to say, however, the corpse that
was to be got well, but mean while the man-planter
himself pegged out, and was buried in the very
bole that he had “digged for his enemy.”
* * *
Joseph Clarke’s prosecution of his old friend
Mrs. Clara Parker for forgery promises to be a
humorous case. The preliminary hearing at
Melbourne City Court has elicited the fact that
portly Joseph “lent” the lady some £BOOO or so,
on the hazy understanding that she was putting it
into a swamp—Kooweerup Swamp. After a few
years of cashing up Joseph got tired, and then his
fair friend squeezed another £550 out of him “ as
a consideration for any claims in an action she
threatened to bring for not going on with the
swamp.” Finally Joseph went down to the
swamp and “found that the property was noth-
ing at all,’ but what sort of property he expected
to find in a swamp didn't come out in evidence.
All he knows for certain is that his money was
swamped. Mrs. Parker used the swamp as a
name to conjure with. “Did she just come and
say ‘ Kooweerup ’ and you would hand her £5OO,
or whatever the amount she wanted ? ” was one of
the questions put to Clarke. “Yes. and more
fool I,” replied the-rich man. Yet, after all, the
exact word employed by lovely woman to raise
specie with is a matter of small consequence.
There are syrens who obtain tens of thousands by
simply beaming on a male friend from time to
time, and saying “Ducky!” If the Pickwickian
expression “chops and tomato sauce” was sus-
ceptible of an amorous interpretation, who can
fathom the depth of mystic meaning in “Koowce-
rup?”—
A ladies' debating society at Cacnnavtlicn
(U.K.) lately discussed the question, “Should
ladies open their husbands’ letters?” After two
nights’ talk, it was resolved by. an overwhelming
majority that they should. That debating society
doesn’t know its business. The real question is,
how ladies are to shut their husbands’ letters again,
so that no one will know the difference. Any
woman can open a man’s letters, but to seal thorn
up again is a work of art.

A Chicago (U.S.A ) World’s Fair agent
who lias been touring N.S.W. combined that
business with the voluntary one of delivering,
temperance lectures. He was a terrific orator
against “ the cursed drink.” All the same, he
spent many days coaxing N.S.W. brewers to send
samples of their “ manufactures ” to the Fair.

“Marriages Solemnised” you always
read this outside of a little bit of a conventicle
shanty, with the starving parson lying perdu
inside, waiting to let the mice into the cage in
exchange for two or three pounds.
* * *
Sydney juries have of late been convicting
everybody presented for trial. Though their
verdicts have been right on the average, just as
the magistrates’ committals are right on the
average, it is now felt that some very great in-
justices have been done during the last few months
by the courts. These cases will occur to anyone
who calmly thinks over the facts of recent financial-
company trials.

The other day Constable John Roy shot
himself dead, at Bondi, Sydney. “A woman ”is
said to have been ‘at the bottom of the trouble.”
Many who read this and who had never before
heard the unfortunate constable’s name will be
interested to learn that Roy was identical with the
particularly handsome blaok-monstaehioed officer
whose lora'c appeared to be about the corner of
King and George-streets, Sydney. A finer-look-
ing fellow than Roy could not bo seen in a day’s
march.

Minister had the key in his pocket. Further m
vestigation revealed the fact that the great states-
man gave out a careful allowance of wood and coal
every morning, and then locked up the shed to
prevent waste. He also gave out the victuals
every morning on the same great principle. Then
the artizan walked downtown, meditating as he
went on the great virtue of Thrift, and borrowed
the key at the potentate’s office, and having
finished his job he locked up the wood and coal
once more, gave back the key, and went away
thinking hard upon many subjects.

A country couple, the other day, called at
a Sydney servants’ registry office, and after wait-
ing their turn for half an hour announced that they
wished to be married. The office-keeper politely
showed them to the proper shop.

They are saying in Tasmania that the
spectacle of any woman rushing for a policeman
in order to forcibly prevent another woman for-
giving a fallen fellow-creature, is a dismal com-
mentary on the horrors of modern Christianity.

Two wildly dishevelled foreigners—H. E.
Seguy, Frenchman, and an anonymous individual
signing himself “ A German ” —are holding a cat-
chorus in Sydney D.T., and abusing each other’s
country gaily. The German began the uproar,
being aggravated thereto by Max O’Rell’s lectures,
and the Frenchman started after him. Both are
fearful examples of “English as she am spoked,”
or rather as she am tried to be spoked under
difficulties. A slab from the German :
A President of the U.S.A. shows his gratitude by receiv-
ing any American at the White House who wishes to shake
hands with him for his successful victory of being elected
as such by them, and as a matter of courtesy acts the
same way to English taxpayers. As a Royalist, as Mnns.
Blouiit expresses himself to be, should ho make such
remarks about princes? Why should they be obliged to
receive English Tax Payers as long as they are doomed
with an independent income and Power before they see
the light of their mother’s bedchamber? .... To
what extent did the Germans sweep the offices for John
Bull at London? If they did, what a glory to them to
divide their time so nicely, and lick the French besides. I
guess at that time the enthusiastic Frenchman was an
English Tax Payer. This great duke refers to Germans
and Swedes, etc., to become the best naturalised citizens
of the States as soon as they are long enough in that
country. Why shouldn’t they ? Whoever is so furious
overjoyed with their native country should stay there,
Mous. Blouet being one of them. . . After all, there is
as much love for liis country in a German as there is in a
Frenchman, which was proved well enough whenever
their Fatherland was subjected to the bloody, unreason-
able furies of the nation they call the French which were
not so awful long ago.
Now for the Frenchman :
Cher Monsieur,—Being a Frenchman, I reaa with in-
terest a letter in yoUr valuable paper of the Glh and
signed “ A Germand.” I cannot let it pass unnoticed, as
it strike me as being a great deal of nonsense in it, and
why Mr. Max O’Rell’s lecture on John Bull should draw
this German out I cannot conceive. In the first place he
ask in what manner John Bull gives the German Princes
out-door relief. Well, if he does not know, he ought to bo
told, and I will leave that for Mr. Max O’Rell to answer,
or any very young English school hoy. He strike me
forcibly that Max O’Rell is as staunch Royalist, hut
lecturing before an audience on the pecularity of a Nation
and showing their dis-Royalty does not prove he is not
loyal, and to prove this practically Max O’Rell was an
officer in the French army and fought for his country in
the Franco-German war. He was not an English tax-
payer as your correspondent said. Possible he a German
at that time was some where in England playing the
trombone in the street and picking up English coppers, or
he was selling contraband cigard at Melbourne nr Sydney.
They do sweep John Bull office at London. What Max
OTlell said is a certainty, and German clerk, it is well
know, leave their country by thousands every month to
seek an existence, not a living, in the Great city of Lon-
don. Max O’Rell being a Frenchman is enough to raise a
German bad nature.
So far, honours are about even.

Parson Brown recently solemnised a
marriage at Bingera (N.S.W.) .Anglican church,
at which only four other persons were present—the

totalled 290 years. The lovers were eacli over GO;
the witnesses each over 70.

A Sydney fool lately received £lO,OOO, the
proceeds of an English legacy. Every third day
he drew £5OO, or, say, £lOOO a week. He gave
hundreds of pounds away to barmaids, and
emerged from the wrestle with his luck, after a 10-
weeks’ contest, with a concertina-adjustment liver,
a parboiled brain, a dishonoured cheque for the
21st £5OO, 47 suits of clothes, 13 watches, F 3
chains, 28 hats, six pairs of “ worked” slippers,
13 garish smoking caps, 111 female photos., 04
unpaid accounts, two bootjacks, eight letters from
bookmakers, five breaeh-of-protnise threats, and
one letter demanding all former epistles. He is
now lurking at street-corners for free drinks.
The Sydney quack “cancer-doctor” Hoods
struck one bad snag in being tried before Acting-
Judge Heydon, and another in having as the
Crown Prosecutor in their case an enthusiastic
sportsman like barrister Coffey. In The Bulle-
tin’s opinion, they struck the third and worst
snag of all by being put into the witness-box by
their counsel” for all they did while there was to
swear themselves into gaol. It is the general
opinion of Sydney lawyers that the Hoods, from
a strictly legal aspect, should have squeezed
through.

Three years ago Abbe Dumoulin, of the
Archdiocese of Aix, France, was convicted of the
murder of Madame Blanchard, a wealthy and
pious member of the congregation, and was tran-
sported “for life” to New Caledonia, chiefly on
the evidence of Kloser, a sexton. Five months
ago, Kioser publicly confessed that he himself was
the murderer, and that he had next day actually
told his crime in Confession to Father Dumoulin
himself. Under the inviolable seal of the Confes-
sional the priest was compelled to submit in
silence to penal servitude on the perjured testi-
mony of the actual murderer. He has since been
relea-ed. Now, ye dramatists !

The quack who collects “testimonials”
and advertises them to boom business, always
gets into worse trouble in the end. The average
testimonial is only a trail leading to the grave of
a ciead patient, and the police find the tombstone
at the end of it.

A Japanese tattoocr has started business
in Cullbis-street, Melbourne. If lie is an artistic
workman tattooing will probably become a tem-
porary rage in that city. Then the fashion will
die out, and a lot of love-sick maidens will spend
the next few years in grief and much sorrow
trying to wash the initials off their arms, and
making very little progress at the job. People
who think of getting tattooed should remember m
time that nothing short of a red-hot poker will
ever obliterate the mark. Memo. Every bank-
cashier who superintends a Sunday-school or sings
in the choir should be tattooed.

What a fascinating book the secret history
of Mount Morgan would make ! Rockhampton
possesses a butcher’s assistant who, three years
ago, was worth eighty thousand pounds in cash.
He was the possessor of a snug butchering estab-
lishment, and had bought a few hundred Morgans
at par. (£1). The shares went up, and he sold out,
only to buy in again ; and so the deal went on.
He was anxious to sell at ills, but his adviser
persuaded him they would see -620 —and he bough t
more, giving bills for the major amount of the
transaction. And then someone pricked the.
bubble, and then crash ! and everything was gone.
The vendor took his other Mount Morgan shares
(lodged as security) for payment of those he had
lately bought, and the one-time .£BO,OOO capitalist
is now an assistant in the butcher s shop that v as
his. His adviser was—but wild horses will not
tear the name from us.
We smile at the well-advertised, but guile-
less, phrenologist who, arguing probably from
what he knows, and not merely from what he sees
in the papers last week, characterised Deeming’s
head as expressive of all evil. What is “ firm-
ness ” in your esteemed friend, the bull-pup-faced
man of nerve, who is ever the most congenial com-
panion of the man of nerves, is “obstinacy,”
brutal, donkeyfied obstinacy, in the fellow you
don’t like. Society can only see unbridled lust
an 1 murderous savagery in the face of the man
who has been convicted of these things. It can
never perceive these things in the face of the man
who sits on the bench. Yet the man whose con n .
tenanco most resembles Deeming’s has a soft se a t
on the judicial bench and enjoys between £lsoo
and .£3OOO a year from the State Treasury of a
British colony. He will probably never get into
the condemned cell, because, as the colonists have
had occasion to notice from time to time, he can, in
the exercise of his “ stern duty to society,” indulge
to the full all the brutal promptings of ids nature.

Two prospectors (a white man and a
Maori), named Hall and Te An, have found a new
lake of great size and beauty among the mountains
of Maori land ; and Hall, who seems to have
jumped the entire honour of tire discovery, pro-
poses that it shall be called Lake Glasgow, im
honour of the new Governor. There is no poetry
in the average groveller’s mean little soul. The
other Maoriland lakes have native names thnn-
sound like sweet music and convey poetic mean-
ings, and among, them the intrusive white clr.-dl
proposes to insert the -harsh-sounding cognomem
of a great, dirty, dark, slush-laden, fog-hauntedl
city that festers by a dank, foul river at the-
other end of the earth. There are not event
any historic associations worth mentioning about:
Glasgow; it is all given up to mud and smells;
and cotton, and great, gaunt, loathsome factories
and there is something horribly incongruous about-
the idea of transferring its name to the glassy
waters and snow-clad peaks of Maoriland. The
man who could be guilty of such an act of van-
dalism would abolish sweet-sounding Manapourii
and re-christen it Porkgoggles, if he had a chance'
to oblige some imported nonentity by so doing,
and he would wipe out the name of grand, solemn,,
old Te Auau, and re-name it Lake Macintosh or
Ilamfat Waters. And when he has successfully
christened his blue inland sea as Lake Glasgow,,
the unfortunate thing is that the name will stick.
The plain, hard-shell Presbyterian whose title is
redolent of the unclean city by the ditch will,
vanish away and bo forgotten, but his uncouth'
designation will keep on for ever. It is a horrible-
thing to think of, and the man who conceived the-
i lea should be exiled to Glasgow straight off, and 1
doomed to live in the Gallowgato for the rest of
Lis days,

building society.

A member of the Melbourne journalists’'
association has his best yarn about a hangman. In a
Victorian town, 200 miles from Melbourne, ibo
prison comprised Jack the Buster and Tassy (from
old Van Diemen’s Land) amid its inmates. Jack
and Tassy were old “t’other side ” pals, and Jack
had to be hanged, but Cobb’s people refused to fetch:
Jack Ketch from Melbourne, because lie bad got
drunk on a former occasion. So Tassy, with many
tears, affectionately hung the Buster—after all the
other prisoners had refused—and shook the dusf-
off his feet with £5 in his pocket.
A Sydney daily paper has printed a half-
column from “A ” who, after being
absent from N.S.W. forSOyears, has returned and
complains that there are people who, at local
Anglican services, bob at the wrong time, or do
not bob at the right time, or bob too much or too ■
little, or something. The abject imbecility of this
kind of thing is about on a par with the monstrous
self-conceit of this brand of pietist in assuming
that the Unknowable cares one jot whether the
public bend their necks, or crook their knees, or
waggle their tails, or stand upon their heads, for
that matter.

We never thought much of the House
Whose rotten foundation is “ birth ; n
Where nobles without any nous
Made laws for a lot of the earth.
We never were struck on the peers
Who govern by family right,
But we hoped for a good many years
Their skins would be passably white.
It never occurred to us that
The time was so nearly at hand
When a nigger as black as your hat
Would rank as a lord of the land.
Great Britons, the same as before,
May boss those ridiculous waves,
But we hardly can hint any move
That none of their mothers were slaves.
The bloated supporters of “ blood ”
Must see what a mess they are in,
Now Hingland has set up a lad
Who sprang from an ebony gin.
Yet worse may be lurking behind ;
Let nothing astonish us now—
For soon we shall probably find
That a duke is the son of a Chow. ’

A lorn succeeds arid morn,
Night closes down
Over the dreary plain,
Over the town.
Vainly for rain-clouds we look night and day,
Vainly for water we earnestly pray ;
Dry are the wells at last,
Foul the supply
Drawn from the bed of creek,
Where carcases lie
Putrid and rotting, t here whitening ribs
Of “ Snowy " or “ Brandy,” ah, would it wcrtiDU5Us.il
Weary of waiting now,
Weary of prayer,
We tall back on whisky
And loudly do swear
At centralisation and Parliament larks
If we have not the rain to dam, why* their,, we’ll, damn.

A British ironmaster recently informed
London Daily News that, in his own furnaces,
the royalty of (is. a ton paid on ironstone amounted
to =£15,000 a year, the whole of which went to the
landlords, while the men who raised the stone
only earned <£7500 a year wages in all. The land-
lord thus gets twice as much for lazing as the
whole of the men together get for sweating.

merit they have. He hears of all
the failures and successes, and can
therefore judge: “I know of no
medicine for Coughs, Sore Throat,
or Hoarseness that had done such ef-
Coughs,
Sore Throat,
Hoarseness,
fective work in my
family as Boschee’s
German Syrup. Last
winter a lady called
at my store, who was
suffering from a very
severe cold. She could hardly talk,
and I told her about German Syrup
and that a few doses would give re-
lief; but she had no confidence in
patent medicines. I told her to take
a bottle, and if the results were not
satisfactory I would make no charge
for it, \ few days after she called
and paid for it, saying that she
would never be without it in future as
a few doses had given her relief. ’ ’ @

Sydney. (Extract from the Era, November 13, 1888:—’
The Automaton “ ALI ” during the lasi month has been
on view at Collins’s Music Hall, Islington. A new Auto-
maton which the inventor, A. De Lacy, has termed
“ALI,” following custom immortal; the figure is dressed
in a turban and Oriental costume, it is worked by elabo-
rate wire works, the motive power being electricity. The
movements are natural and lifelike, and paradoxical
as it may seem it has not the appearance of being an
Automaton. “ALI” turns the leaves of the Koran,
becomes warlike and goes through sword exercise, plays
tambourine and hells, and altogether completely succeeds
in engrossing ths attention of the audience, in interesting
them, and winning loud applause. The whole network of
wire, by which the movements of I lie figure arc deter-
mined, is exposed by the Inventor, Mr, Dp Lacy, «ln
must have taken years to have brought his mechanical
figure to pci fed ion.

HE BOUGHT A SORSE
IV/TY dear,’’ began Mr. Cityman, as he
entered bis suburban home the othe r
evening in apparently high spirits, “I did a good
stroke of business to-day.” “ Indeed ! And what
was that?” archly inquired Mrs. C,, with a well-
meant though unsuccessful attempt to appear
interested. “Nothing short of a dead bargain.
I am going to utilise the old stable where the fowls
are housed, and I mean to ride into town every
day. It will benefit my health, and I won’t have
to hang around the streets waiting for a’bus.”
“So you have bought a horse then?” was the acid
rejoinder. “Of course I have ; you don’t suppose
l cl buy a donkey, or a mule, or a dromedary, or a
locomotive, or ” “ Well,” broke in Mrs. C. in
conciliatory tones, “I didn’t know that you
could manage a horse, and besides, I should have
thought—” “Oh, certainly,” snapped out Mr.
Cityman, “you’re always thinking instead of
making sure. But when you see me coming in
after a rattling good canter you’ll change your
tune. 1 tell you this horse is a beauty, and no
mistake, quiet as a child, no vice about him, good
pacer, and warranted not to shy or cut up capers.”
Jtarly the following morning the steed was
brought home and handed over, and the proud
owner, after several desperate tries, finally
succeeded in climbing into the saddle and set off
for town. There was no wind to speak of that
d;iy, and a small boy on the wayside was frantic-
ally endeavouring to persuade a reca’citrant kite
into a recognition that it was destined for a
nobler career than scraping up road metal, when
a spry horse and a perspiring, doubled-up
equestrian hove in sight. There was a busy
eX u C ’ t v llg . t,irie . or a b°ut three minutes, during
which the air was filled with profanity and
buttons and things, and then a riderless horse
might be seen ambling peacefully down the road
as if nothing had happened. Mr. Cityman took
thene_xt bus home, where he humbly submitted
himself for repairs. These his wife soon effected
with the aid of a valuable family remedy always
kept in stock by her. The next day a horse was
advertised “for sale, cheap,” and Mr. C., while
distinctly disclaiming all desire to “make an
advertisement of himself,” felt bound to admit
that his wife was right again when she preached
the doctrine that St. Jacobs Oil for the absolute
relief and cure of contusions, sprains, and bruises
knows no rival. All chemists and storekeepers
sell it.

The Wild-Cat Column.
Heaven grant t'kat shortly some attempt will be
made to clear two-thirds, or at least one-half, of the
mining men, so-called, that loaf about Pitt-street, Sydney,
between King and Bridge Streets. Times have changed,
and so have they. There was a time when they were
more or less excited, but now they amble aimlessly,
dejectedly, about; going from the P.O. Chambers to the
Empire Hotel only to meander listlessly back again. Their
clothes are getting rusty and beastly familiar to the
much-bled public, whose eye revolts at the sight of these
wild, hungry spiders standing at the doors of their webs.
A rush to any new gold-field would cause a temporary
exodus, another boom would agitate them, and in time
they would be resplendent in fine linen and raiment that
would be a transformation. There is a tremendous field
in Africa for them if they could only get there. A scheme
to take, say 50, there would receive the writer’s cordial
■support. Besides, there’s money in it! Strangers in a
strange land, they would take the residents in and boom
things. Australia could spare them, at any rate until
next boom. They consist largely of “mining experts;”
most of them are lazy humbugs; and nearly all now arc
brokers of the stoniest description.
a # *
Here we are again. Faulkner's, P ainbula, N.S.W.,
reports The double whip-hauling gear is a perfect
success and a great boon.” It must be obvious that the
future of that field bangs on a whip-pole, and now that
Faulkners have gone in for a double one —it must have cost
£s—dividends are assured. The public will watch the
progress of that “great boon ” with breathless anxiety.
# # *
It is good news to hear that little Louis Beyers
has at last struck it again at Hill End—this time in Pax-
ton’s old claim, of which lie is manager and one of the
largest shareholders. It is premature to say whether the
discovery is a patch or a permanent run of gold, but if it
is true that it has been made some distance from the old
hanging-wall and in a part of the country never before
intersected, it may lie the means of reviving the grand old
field which returned some nine tons of gold from the
smallest quantity of stone known in any part of the
world.
# * *
The Garden Gully United is again to the front
heading the Bendigo yields. For many years it never
passed a fortnight without a dividend ; its shares were at
pence, jumped, and remained at pounds for some ten
years ; after tons of gold had been raised they receded to
shillings, and inasmuch as the present crushings are
coining from a big body at a great depth shareholders are
having visions of"another long dividend-paying period.
# * *
Perhaps the truest fissure reef in New South
Wales is that of Mitchell’s Creek Co., a few miles
out of Wellington. It has an underlie of 45
degrees. The formation is diorite, the ore quartz con-
taining, below water level, five to ten per cent, of copper,
iron, and arsenical pyrites. They never see a speck of
free gold, although ordinary crushing liberates half-
an-oz.; the balance, up to an oz. to the ton, is obtained
from the pyrites. It is a 640-acre freehold, owned by four
men, who have obtained during the last 18 months some
£20,000 worth of gold at a good profit. The last lift, at a
depth of 500 feet, is looking all right for a long continu-
ance of good crushing. It appears to be a long run of
gold as distinguished from narrow shoots—hence the
value of the property.
# * *
The Hazellmrst mine at Parkes, N.S.W., con-
sists of an acre, in which there was, up to some months
ago, a rattling shoot of rich gold, out of which the Orange
school of mining speculators expected to make a big
thing. Hazelhurst, the former holder, after making some
thousands, sold it to the Orange crowd for £16,000, and
they have been sadly left with it.
# * *
The curse of N S.W. mining is the amount of
capital for which most of its mines are floated; of this
the greed of vendors and the gullibility of the public are
the causes. To satisfy the owners with an enormous
bundle of paid-up scrip, the “under offer’’-man with
another bundle, some for the brokers, provisional direc-
tors, and other middlemen, the capital is made £lOO,OOO.
At last—vears too late—that great animal, the public, has
awakened to the gruesome fact that it requires 12500z5.
of refined gold over and above the quantity required for
working and managerial expenses to pay one shilling
dividend on £lOO,OOO capital, and that the bulk of the
capital on which the shilling dividend has to be paid is a
fraud, a delusion, and a snare. Not one gold mine in a
thousand when floated justifies £lOO,OOO capital. It is a
monstrous swindle, engendered by the idea that the
public, when buying, seldom ask the amount of capital,
and therefore there is no proportionate difference in the
price per share whether £50,000 or £lOO,OOO. At Bendigo,
by usa<>-e, not by law, they have a customary capital of
£24,000. Why?' Because every penny represents exactly
£lOO-e.g., sixpehnv call or dividend, £6OO. Every
attempt to increase" it has been successfully opposed.
Would that N.S.W. had taken a leaf out of the Bendigo
hook. It will in time, and the sooner the better.
* * %
A tale of a Northern mine that, during a boom,
went up like a rocket and came down like a stick. It was
placed on the market with a supposed capital of £lOO,OOO
represented by 200,000 shares of 10s. each, 80,000 being
paid up, and the remaining 120,000 offered at 6d. per
share on application and Od. on allotment. As a noted
firm of boss manoeuvrers was prominently identified with
the scheme, lots of genuine small men applied tor shares,
sending ii deposits ranging from £5O to ±lOO. Believing
that a boom could he worked in a fashion similar to that
which had frequently been successful, the promoters
hluffingly relumed most of these deposits, declaring that
the shares had all been rushed for and had been allotted.
In due time, the local exchange quoted the shares,
“Buyers, 3s. ; sellers, 3s. 6d. ;” and sales were reported
as having been made. Now was the orthodox time fot the
harvest \ hut, somehow or other, the bottom had dropped
out of the boom, and the original genuine customers
didn’t bite at the expected 3s. or 4s. per share which was
to have taken the place of the “fid. on application and
fid. on allotment.” Later, the shares went down to zero.
Later still, 29,000 of them that were supposed to he for-
feited were bought in at auction by a boomers’ jackal for
3d. and Id. per share ; and, yet later, 34,904 were taken by

* * *
On an Australian goldfield not so very long ago,
several alleged goldmines went bung simultaneously,
their total liabilities amounting to £350. A legal firm
which bossed their liquidation managed to squeeze about
£3750 out of the sqiie zahle shareholders, who were
harassed by every artifice known to law—and mining law
at that. Many decent citizens saw the inside of a gaol
in consequence of inability or unwillingness to comply
with tiie terms of judgments. Yet, not more than some
£5O ever found its way into the pockets of the creditors
of the bust-up companies.
# # *
The N.S.W. Supreme Court decision against
Mount Chalmers (Q.) Gold-mining Co., which was a N.S.W.
registered no-liability company mining in Queensland
really amounts to a ruling that mere shareholders in such
a co. have no legally sustainable interest whatever in it;
that, in short, it is personally and collectively owned by
the directors. In the case of a golden-gallened claim,
this would be perditionally rough on common shareholders,
and a glorious pool for the directors. However, the rule
cuts both ways. If the directors own the mine, they are
also legally responsible for all tire debts of the alleged
no-liability co., and, if the claim turns out a duffer, they
..can lie compelled to fork out, if they are worth powder
and shot.” The Act suggests several schemesEithei
make all shareholders directors; or else select assured
paupers as directors. By the first, all would equally sink
or swim ;by the second, the “company could laugh at
its creditors. After all, the simplest plan would he a
Federal agreement between the various Australian pro-
vinces, that every mining company must be registered
■ within the province in which it prosecutes mining opeia-
. tions. _ * ,

Englishman said : “May I presume to ask your highness
if, during the course of your recent peregrinations
through the vast interior, you have ever observed any
surface indications of a mineralogical formation that
would lead you to surmise that they conceal auriferous
deposits?” The obliging blackfellow instantly responded,
with a melodious laugh, “Yowi ; brudder belong ame
catohem kangaroo. Plenty you gib it bacca

The new smelter at Argenton, West coast of
Tasmania, has been christened the Lady Hamilton. It is
to he hoped she will be more free with her silver than her
godmother.
* # *
When Sir Robert Hamilton recently visited the
silver mines on the Tasmanian West coast, the miners
“chalked his feet” with traditional care, but His Ex.
never responded with a single shilling. His economical
style did not impress the sons of toil “deep in the dark-
some mine/* and one or two thought his elongated
secretary might answer up hotter. But it was all in vain.
As one miner said, “both of the beauties has had their
galena pinched out.”
# # *
What has become of the Bonang Co. ? asks a
sickened shareholder. It is dead, although an attempt at
its resurrection is about to be made. Its history Short,
rich shoot of quartz, water troublesome, capital £lOO,OOO
—four times too much. £lO,OOO spent on surface works—
twice as much as required—-crushing plant beyond
capacity of the mine, chlorination works started but never
completed—altogether only another evidence of over-
capital, bad management, extravagance, exaggeration and
the fact that you cannot make a good big mine out of a
good little one.
* * *
Zeehan to-day is what Silverton was a few years
ago —partly comprised of “ conundrums.” Each wanders
how the other fellow does it; but, so far, the police have
not enforced the “ invisible means of support ” act.

Some years ago, in a Maoriland court, an exile
from County Donegal appeared and applied for a warrant
for the apprehension of a person who had “taken him
down ”in a raining swindle. “ What Was the name of the
mine, Mr. Dougharty ?” asked the clerk.
“Dunno!” said Moichal. “Where was it situated?”
“Dunno.” “ Well, 1 cannot issue a warrant unless you
can give me some particulars. Cannot you tell me any-
thing?” “Oi kin tell ye the colour aV it.” “ The colour
of it 1” said the astonished functionary. “ Yis, the colour
av it. It wuz a purty pink piece av ground with a nate
grane bovther round it, an’ wid something like hairy
caterpillars crawlin’ on it, begorra.” Poor Mike had seen
the plan.

in another province. Considering the number of mining
oo.’s registered in Melbourne which work N.S.W.-located
mines, the latter province is getting its watermelon tapped
at both ends.

A story from Broken Hill— probably apocryphal:
The underground boss of the “graveyard shift,” i.e., that
working from midnight to eight a.m., was much worried
by the men in out-of-the-way corners of the mine sleeping
instead of working. One night he caught two of them
snoring placidly, and after covering them plentifully with
candle-grease, kicked them into a state of wakefulness and
sent them home. Next day one of the delinquents depu-
tationised the general manager. “Well, what’s the
matter, my man,” he was asked. “It was a fair catch
last night, boss, and I suppose it means (he sack !
“ What do you mean, Andy,” said the manager to whom
the occurence had not been reported. “Oh,” replied
Andv, “shift-boss caught me asleep, right enough, hut
twarnt my fault; it was Jimmy’s turn to watch for the
boss coming, and danged if he didn’t go to sleep, toe.”

When bound to the stake, the nigger burnt
alive the other day in Arkansas said, just ere the
woman appointed to do the deed applied the
match to bis kerosened clothing: “Stand back,
gentlemen, and let the ladies see.”
About ten more “Anarchist outrages ” by
means of dynamite and otherwise have been
cabled from Europe since last week, and
yet nobody lias been killed. Also a dozen
more alleged “bombs” have been discovered
by the police before they had time to blow
up. There is a very large moral just here.
The police, in three cases out of four, find the
Anarchists’ bomb before it explodes—in other
words, either the police put the bomb there them-
selves, and therefore knew where to find it,
or the “Anarchist” who put it there was a
hired spy who at once gave information, or there
isn’t any bomb at all, or else, when an object
about the size of a saucer is placed at random any-
where in a city of, perhaps, 800,000 population, the
police are so superhumanly intelligent that, in 75
percent, of the cases, they get on its track and
run it down in the three minutes or so which inter-
venes between the lighting of the slow fuse and: the
explosion. Now, there is absolutely nothing more
to guide the police to the location of a bomb than
to that of a dishcloth. Let anybody plant 20 dish-
cloths at random about London or Paris, and
count how many of them the police find within
three minutes, and if they don’t discover at least
ten, then the Anarchist cablegrams are so many
self-evident lies.

“There is to-day no German literature,
there is only a Jewish literature written in the
German language. Most of the London dailies
are represented by Jews both at Berlin and
Vienna. The German intellect is devoted to the
sword. The Jew wields the pen. The journalist
is despised in Germany alike by Emperor and by
Socialist.”— Charles Low.

across this idea of the origin of the black s
boomerang or a better one ? Place a dry brigalow

and “ come-back ” as in the real article.

Who wrote the burlesque petition, on
Deeming’s behalf, to the Executive Council i It
is signed “Marshall Lyle,” but reads like Deakin
in mufti. Worthy of the old masters in humour
is the argument that the prayer of Hodges—“ May
the Lord have mercy on your soul! ’—means that
his Honour desires to meet Deeming in heaven,
and that therefore more time should be allowed to
prepare such soul for eternity. Without the
I streaks of sarcasm, the petition might have proved
effective. The lawyers want editing.

A Wayside Queen.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
n VaS k° ni hi the season of fire—
When a mantle of murkiness lay
a S^ lu lon k of the crimson destroyer—
And none knew the name of her sire,
But the woman, and she, ashen gray.
In the fierce pangs of motherhood lay.
The skies were aflame at her coming
With a marvellous message of ill,
And fear-stricken pinions were drumming
-*■ o? '!°b heavy air, whence the humming
Ofjnsectsrose, sudden and shrill,
As they fled from that hell-begirt hill.
the smoke-serpent writhed in her tresses,
Ihe flame kissed her hard on the lips,
ohe smiled at their ardent caresses—
As the wanton who smiles, but represses
A lover’s hot haste, and so slips
t rom the arm that would girdle her hips.
Such the time of her coming and fashion;
How long ere her day shall bo sped,
And she goes to rekindle past passion
With langourous glances that flash on
The long-straightened limbs of the dead,
Where they lie in a winter-wet bed ?
Where the wide waves of evergreen carry
The song—sad and soft—of the surge
To feathered battalions that harry
The wizen-armed bloodwoods that tarry
lor ever, chained down on the verge
Of a river that mutters a dirge.
’Tis a dirge for the dead men it mutters,
Those weed-entwined strangers, who lie
With the drift, in the whirlpools and gutters—
Swol’n hand or a garment that flutters
Wan shreds as the waters rush by,
And the flotsam, froth-freckled, rides high.
Is it there that she buries her lovers,
i This woman in scarlet and black ?
Those swart Caballeros, the drovers,
What sov’reignty set they above hers,
Riding in by a drought-beset track
To a fate which is worse than the rack.
A queen, no insignia she weareth,
Save the lustrous crown of her-hair,
Her beauty, the sceptre she beareth ;
For men and their miseries careth
As little as tigresses care
For the quivering flesh that they tear.
She is sweet as white peppermint flowers,
And harsh as red gum when it drips
From the heart of a hardwood that towers
Straight up, she hath marvellous powers
To draw a man’s soul through his lips
With a kiss like the stinging of whips.
Warm nights, weighted down with wild laughter
When sex is unsexed and uncouth,
Is the chorus that climbs to the rafter,
No thought of the days to come after—
She has little regret and less ruth,
As she tempts men to murder their youth.
Is she marked down as yet by the flaming
Great eye of the Righter of Wrong?
How long ere the Dreaded One, claiming
His due, shall make end of our shaming?
“How long, Mighty Father, how long?”
Is our wearisome burden of song.

day sent its collector round to the Insolvency
Court to take up a dividend declared in a certain

Queensland “ conspiracy ” prisoners (unionist

It is persistently asserted that, whatever
may be the results of the two N.S.W. Railway
Commissions now sitting, Chief Commissioner
Eddy intends to resign his post very shortly. He
is said to be sick of being a dummy autocrat who
is a free target for any mudpelter.
Napoleon was one day searching for a book
and discovered it on a shelf above his reach.
Marshal Moncey, who was present—one of the
tallest men in the army—stepped forward, saying;
“Permit me, sire, I am higher than your majesty.’
“ You are longer, marshal,” said the emperor, With
a frown.
A young Melbourne solicitor sat discon-
solately in his top-flat, sky-palace, Melbourne
office, last week. There was a tap at the door,
and in walked a well-dressed lady with a little
boy. He bowed her to a seat and waited to hear
her case. She didn't seem in a hurry, but started
to talk weather, Deeming, and so on. Then she
narrated her whole family history, while the boy
climbed all over the office furniture, and spilt the
ink. At last she said she really must go, and
asked “Alfio” to “come off the table”—which
“Alfie” did, breaking a pair of vases in his
descent. The attorney smiled faintly, and said it
didn’t matter, and seeing her about to depart,
enquired what business she had come to see him
upon. She seemed tremendously surprised but
went on to explain : “Alfie, you see, was never in
a lift before, so I brought him up to this floor, and
as I was rather tired, I thought you wouldn’t
object to letting me rest for a while. Besides,
Skinnem & Grinder, down below, are my
solicitors.” That was all.
Flain English..

When you are suffering from indigestion no good
can result from taking tonics or stimulants, wliich
create a false appetite and conduce to an overload-

organs are incapable of performing their proper
functions. If a stimulant is fancied the desire can
be effectually, but only temporarily, satisfied by
taking a teaspoonful of brandy diluted with water,
but if a permanent cure for indigestion is desired
take Boot's Indigestion Cure, which is not a “cure
all claim big to be a specific for every epidemic
that arises—but is an absolute cure for Indigestion
only.

Bishop Barlow, of Queensland, made a
progress up North recently, and the faithful got
up deputations and read addresses to him at every
port lie came to. At one place, however, the
steamer anchored five miles off shore, late one
cold, damp night, and the principal lights of the
church, who had been shivering on the muddy
shore for two hours with the address, hired a
lighter and went off to meet her. They expected
to find the bishop waiting on deck for them, so
that they could gabble over the document and go
back home again ; but when they reached the ship
he was in bed, and there was no steward aboard
who could point out his cabin, and the deputation
had to wait on deck till morning. There was a
drizzling rain that night, and the deck was cold
and hard, and the lighterman’s bill kept mounting
up every hour as he waited, but still the deputa-
tion stood its ground all through the small Hours
of the morning with grim resolution. Then at
five o’clock they learned that the steamer was
about to proceed on its way, so they burst into
the bishop’s cabin, and read their address to
him in bed, and ho answered, with one eye shut
and the other sleepily half closed, that it was the
happiest moment of his life, and thereupon the
whistle blew, and the deputation escaped over the
side into their lighter again and got home, and
the prelate fell asleep once more, and the ceremony
was over.
Last week Mary Ann Elizabeth Ryckman,
of Melbourne, being aggravated by the fact that
her small boy bad had a disagreement with the
small boy of Mrs. Chatfield, who lived in the
same street, threw dynamite into that lady’s house
and nearly blew the legs off Mr. Chatfield, who
was removed in a horribly mangled state to the
hospital. Then, just as the police were coming to
arrest her, she exploded another cartridge in her
own premises, blew her hand off and most of her
hair and features, reduced half her cottage
to ruins, covered the constable with bricks and
debris, and was finally picked out of the
wreckage, half-dead and apparently crazy, and
carried away on an ambulance. If Mrs. Mary
Ann Elizabeth Ryckman had been located in
Europe she would have been cabled out as another
fearful outrage by anarchists, accompanied by
numerous arrests, but, being near at hand, she
proves to be only an aggravated woman who got
unduly excited about a row between two
small boys. Distance lends enchantment to
the anarchist as well as to many other things.
As Sirs. Chatfield was absent from home when
the blast went off her enemy’s scheme partially
missed fire, but still Mrs. Ryckman has met with
a certain measure of suosess. Sir. Chatfield,
judging by present appearances, will be afflicted
with a wooden leg for the remainder of liis life,
and if Sirs. Ryckman doesn’t go hopelessly mad,
or die, or get penal servitude for life, she will bo
able, when she comes out of gaol, to lean over the
fence and shout “Peg-leg!” at her discomfited
victim. Site will have a wooden arm with a hook
at the end of it herself, but there is no ointment
without a fly in it somewhere, and the hook will
come in handy to hang on to the fence with.
Way back in the “Sixties” a Sydney
suburban R.G. church was in charge of a priest
whose name might have been Father John Si‘Nab
-—but it wasn’t. With him stayed a new-chum
nephew, Michael, who was waiting for something
to turn up, and meanwhile turned down more
spiritual comfort than was good for what he used
to term his “ locus shtand-eyc.” Ills uncle, know-
ing the strength of Slichael’s “ wakeness, ” kept
the strong waters under lock and key, and Sirs.
O’Dowd, the housekeeper, was warned never to
allow the keys into Michael's possession under
penalty of the purgatorial fires. One sweltering
hot day Michael, having spent his ’bus allowance
in the city on “cordials,” was compelled to “fut
it ” home, where he duly arrived with the thirst
of Tantalus upon him. Knowing that it was use-
less to plead for even a “ tainte ” from Mrs.
O’Dowd, he was about to draw on the pump
when a knock was heard at the front door,
on opening which Michael was met by a cleric
who introduced himself as Archdeacon M'En-
croe, and who was accordingly ushered into
the parlour with the most profuse ceremony.
Archdeacon M'Encroe was the Father Mathew of
Australia, and even surpassed the Irish reformer
in the vigour of his denunciations of the “accursed
drink.” Michael knew nothing of this, but,
clutching the golden opportunity, he rushed to
the housekeeper, told her the illustrious visitor
was “famishin’ for a dhrink,” and succeeded in
obtaining a big bottle of beer and another of prime
mountain dew. He had poured out a bumper of
beer for himself and one for his guest, when the
latter, thrusting his glass aside, said, “My young
friend, shun that stuff as you would the very devil
himself.” This was a bad set-back for Michael,
but he was now desperate, and so, raising his
beaker, he said, “ Oi’ll think about that, yer
rivirince, but as this is the first time Oi’ve had
the pleasure of seein’you, Oi raley musht dhrink
your very good health, sir.” He did, too, and
soon the Archdeacon left. An hour or two later
both bottles were empty, but Michael was as full
as the great tun of Heidelberg, and the house-
keeper, dreading the wrath to come, hustled him
off to bed and cleared away the wreck. Having a
presentiment that something was wrong, Father
John roused the slumbering hero up on his return,
and asked—“ Was there anyone here during my
Absence?” “ There was,” replied Michael, as he
sat up and endeavoured to pull himself together—
“ The Archdacun was here but he didn't shtop
long.” “ Indeed,” said the uncle, “I’m sorry I
was out, but I trust you did the honours of the
house during ray absence.” “Faith Oi did that
same,” returned Michael bravely. Father John
saw bow the battle had raged, and being a bit of
a wag he continued ; ‘ This is a matter which
touches me deeply, Michael. I do trust that you
showed the Archdeacon that he was in the hospit-
able house of an Irish gentleman.” “Lukhere,”
shouted the guileless nephew, who felt that he
had done the right thing, after all, “ I poured th’
dhrink into him until at lasht he pushed his glass
and said, ‘ Young man, don’t pressh me any more,
for if Oi dhrank another dhrop, begorra Oi’d
bnsht.’” “The Archdeacon said that, Michael,
did he ? ” “ Thitn was his very words.” “ I am
delighted to hear he was in such good form,”
returned Father John ; “ now lie down and finish
your sleep, and when you wake read this little
pamphlet of the Archdeacon’s and you will see
what a brilliant man he is.” At dinner next day
Father John carelessly remarked, “Clever man,
the Archdeacon, is he not ? ” But Michael, who
had mastered the pamphlet headed “Drink and
Damnation,” said he didn’t feel as well as a young
man should, and retired to kick himself in the
privacy of his own chamber.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
When Walter Reeks, the yacht-designer, came
out to Sydney some years since he was “knocked of a heap”
when he saw the models of the Xavifa and Australian, the
yachts built a life-time ago by Dan Sheehy. Mr. Reeks
declared that Mr. Sheehy was 30 years before the old-
couritry builders, who would at the present day look upon
the Xarifa and Australian as perfection in model. The
old Australian is still in Port Jackson, and, properly
fitted out, could now knock lumps off anything of her
tonnage. It is an interesting fact that the keel of the
Australian is a part of the wrecked Dunbar.
* # #
Dear Bulletin, —You ask in your last issue,
“ What sort cf a sensation would a female aquatic cham-
pion make?” The question is quite unnecessary; there
never will be any such champion—at least, not unless she
gets her hair cut very short. The fatal point about the
sculling female is that she has to stop every quarter of a
mile to take her hair out of her mouth, and that unfor-
tunate delay spoils her chances every time. Even when
she does her hair up in a hard knot on top of her head it
invariably conies down in about five minutes, and then it
gets into her throat, and she tries vainly to Spit it out,
and while she is struggling with it her opponent gels hull
down on the horizon. —Yours, A - c., Titus Salt.
# %
Sculler Searle’s monument —a broken column-
called forth the exclamation from a passenger on the
Stanhury-Sullivan steamer—“ What a shame to break
poor Searle’s monument like that! ”
* # #
The totalisator is gradually killing the Adelaide
bookmakers. Whilst you couldn’t get more than 10 to 1
at the outside from the layers, the “tote” was panning
out dividends giving 50 per cent, better returns than the
books, offered. Had The Despised, which came third, won
Adelaide Cup, the dividend would have been £lOO. This
;s what will kill the ringmen.
* * *
Outsiders cannot quite fathom the desire of
Sydney Tattersall’s Club to dispense with the services of
Mr. Scarr as handicapper, and a numerously-signed peti-
tion is going the rounds asking for a reconsideration of
the matter. Mr. Scarr, an experienced gentleman whose
honour is unchallengeable, happens to he one of the very
few institutions in connection with Sydney racing in
which race-goers have any confidence at a time when the
“sport of kings ” fairly stinks in the nostrils of straight-
going men. A change just now will he ill-advised unless
good cause can be shown.
# * #
As already mentioned, the Queensland feather-
weight champion is a Kanaka. And the leading bowler
in the Cooparoo Cricket Club (runners up for the Banana-
land premiership) is Harry Murray, a christianised native
of Mallicolo.
#* v *
A certain bantam pugilist of renown is solemnly
warned to seize bichloride of gold by the forelock, for the
hiss of the serpent is at hand. Nearly every night, he
raises a satanic corrobhoree in some Sydney bar, and in
some cases the police are too much afraid of the “ push ” to
interfere. Moreover, when drunk he sings. He prowls
about peaceful neighbourhoods at I and 2 a.m., and his
anthem is like the curse of a jilted hyena, and his hal-
lelujah like the war-cry of a tom-cat climbing the moon-
light. He had better —hie—knock all these habits off.
Surely the churning of milk bringeth forth butter, and
the wringing of the nose of the whisky-puncher shall he
done by a little child.
* * *
After all his fabulous winnings at Monte Carlo,
Mr. “Bonne Chance” Wells, of whom Australia has
heard so much in cablegrams, does not appear to be pos-
sessed of much cash, for he has been sued hy an English
lady to recover £5O, which he obtained from her osten-
sibly for a share in some wonderful patent, hut really,
she says (and probably with truth), for the purpose of
gambling. #
Who would not be a prominent Melbourne foot-
baller? Secretary Marshall, of the V.F.A. says that the
powerful M.C.C. (which now runs the M.F.C.) has a large
number of prominent footballers (from other clubs) em-
ployed about its various grounds. They are popularly
supposed to keep the sparrows from eating the newly-
sown grass-seed. In the cricket-season they are employed
in the manly and active occupation of going round the
ground with a stick, to prevent small boys from climbing
over the fence. And yet these men would hit you with a
brick if you suggested" that they were professionals.
# * *
The next interprovincial sculling and eight-oar
hoatraces are fixed for Brisbane River on June 1. The
funds of the Victorian crew are so low that they may not
be able to attend.

between Williams and Hatcher, “ Athletic Contest.”

# # *
A N.Y. sport has been disqualified for attach-
ing a portable electric battery to his horse. This instru-
ment was fixed to the saddle, and the current was
conveyed through the spurs. Whenever these were
applied to the horse’s sides that horrified animal ran like
aprad possessed by the devil. When the owner didn’t
want to win he left the battery at home, and the amazing
difference in the record made by the electrified beast,
and the same beast when he wasn’t electrified, at last drew
attention, and led to an inquiry.

Touch a man’s pocket and you wound him in his
sorest spot. The habitues of Randwick and the Driving
Park are littledifferent from other men, and unless a radica
change takes place in the working of certain notorious,
stables at both places mentioned, the writer predicts a
disturbance some day that will create a best on record for
Australia in the riot line.
X he New York Wheel, the cyclists’organ, thus
discourses upon athletes generally;—“lt is written in
‘The Book’ that the man who relies for success on
physical effort or physical exploit is doomed to disinte-
gration, morally, mentally, socially ; yes, even financially,
though that is the least of it. There are exceptions, of
course. We shall ignore the exceptions, few and far
between, and point at the scores of cases that prove the
rule. We take examples from all classes, those who have
competed for glory and those who have competed as a
means of livelihood. First, recall to your mind all the
notable athletes, cycle racing-men, running-men, walkers,
runners, jumpers, &c., that 3 011 have ever known, and
judge them by the sreial laws of success. Take, tben, the
list of champion sprint-runners of the world, all the way
from Big Foot Johnson down to our modern Mercury,
Harry Hutchens. Take them all, amateurs andnrofis-
sionals, and mark what they are and where they are. Go,
then, to your record of lighting-men, from the earliest
dog-eat-dog days of the Nottingham Pet and Birmingham
Spider down to our Solid ans and Cardiffs, and mark what
and where they are. Study the University boat-lists, and
learn the lesson of our early decay, of middle-life ob-
scurity, of after-life senility. First, some English cyclists;
some pure amateurs, some makers’ amateurs, some
professionals. The two brightest particular stars of the
path both dead in foreign countries, two convicted cf
theft, one other noted amateur a physical wreck, another
a another thoroughly tricky and crooked and
a world-wanderer, three keepers of ‘pubs.,’ the Mecca of
so many champions, four others lost in the Coventry
factories, mere bench hands, two others slaves in the coal
mines. We fail to recall five men who have ever done
anything worth anything; not more than five men who
have ever grown into decent members of society. And
now, back across the water, to onr own people, both
amateurs and professionals:—Three dead, three turned
barkeepers, one convicted of murder and afterwards shot,
three retired from all exercise by medical instructors on
account of weak hearts, another dead in a foreign country,
five retired to the humblest sort of private life, three
noted national fakirs and hippodroming showmen. Could
we extend the list ? Well, rather. But we will haste to
repeat the lesson which we have tried to illustrate, notably,
that along the line of physical supremacy lies mediocrity ;
that in the trail of physical exertion, as a means of liveli-
hood, lies moral and social rottenness and general failure ;
that one may become a champion with credit and honour,
but that remaining a champion invites a peril. We want
to enforce this lesson upon ambitious amateurs who may
pay momentary attention to those writers who have the
entree to a dishonest and degraded press, and who are
prating learnedly of cash prizes and flying starts and other
sensations.” And isn’t all this true of” Australia?

Tom King, the clipping trotter which finished
first at Sydney Driving Bark, on Friday, was hawked
round for sale at 20 sovs. before Harry Knight discovered
he could trot. It would take a few scores to buy him now.

Another big smash-up on Saturday in the
Flying Handicap, at Warwick Farm. Bonnette, when in
the lead, fell, four horses fell over her, and nearly all the
rest of the field either ran over or jumped the fallen nags
and jockeys. Gainsford, off Plain Bill, had his collar-
bone broken.

# # #
Calcutta Englishman tells a cricketing yarn
of a batsman spooning a hall vigorously out Of sight, and
effecting a run before it dropped to earth again close to
the wicket, on to which it then bobbed, he thus having
played the hall on to his wicket, and being technically
howled out although he scored a run at the same time.
