A Maoriland sporting writer declares that the
reason why Maoriland jockeys are as unpopular as China-
men in Australia is that the three islands can hold their
own with the big continent in regard to both first-class
horses and horsemen.

The withdrawal of Orme (which was only
poisoned by a had tooth, ic now appears) from the Two
Thousand Guineas, cleared the way for the victory of a
blood relation. Bonavista, the winner, is also a son of
the Duke of Westminster’s famous horse, Bend Or, and a
great grandson of Stockwell. Mr. Rose, the owner of
Bonavista, is a new name, real or assumed, in the English
racing calendar.
At Richmond (Melb.) races the other day, the
judge decided that “Claudia had won by half a nose.”
“ Won by a nostril ” is a more concise formula.
A Queenslander accepts as a compliment
the title Bananalander. A colonist of the “ three
islands ” is proud to be called a Maorilander.
Victorians feel jerky when styled Gumsuckers.
N. S, Welshmen are indifferent when dubb. 1
Cornstalks. South Australians “get the hump”
when addressed as Croweaters. North Tasmanians
resent being nicknamed Mutton birds ; South Tas-
manians boil over if designated Barracoota.; while
all Tasmanians would (whenever able) wipe out in
blood the insult Vandemonians. West Austra-
lians have varied views about the method of
accepting the epithet Crocodiles.
The Mayor of Grenfell, N.S.W., has
stepped out of his pigeonhole, in the name of the
citizens within his mayoralty, to howl for the
blood of Deeming, and lias urged on the execution
in a frenzied telegram to the Victorian Crown Law
authorities. This is a legitimate step in the duo
self-assertion of (1 renfell as the hub of the universe.
Its next move will be to instruct President Carnot
to guillotine the French Anarchist, Ravachol. It
is 1 ko a frog advising a comet to destroy an
offensive planet. Humanity, in its future
struggles, must keep one eye on God and
the other on Grenfell, provided some marauding
midnight Chinaman doesn't meanwhile carry off
the whole municipality as a load of manure.
Dear Bulletin,—The par in your last
issue re the sale of George Anson’s books, reminds
me of a little incident in book-auctioneering,
which took place in Sydney not 50 years ago. A
literary man was going “home” and his library
was advertised for sale, among the books being
about 2000 uncut volumes. All Sydney turned up
at the sale and the books brought fancy prices.
It afterwards leaked out that the bulk of the
volumes were the surplus stock of a leading book-
seller who made use of the opportunity to clear
out his cellars. The pathetic part of the business
was that several heavy purchasers sought the
bookseller’s advice at the sale as to buying and his
answer in every case was—“ The rarest lot of
books I’ve ever seen—buy ’em.” The bookseller
can’t well be blamed if the public were “ had,” as
a man with one eye could have seen through the
2000 uncut volumes part of the “ad.”—H.B.
Premier Shiels has arranged a treaty of
peace between Judge Hodges and ex-Attorney-
General Duffy, on the G. N. Taylor question.
Hodges will say no more in explanation, and
Duffy, having resigned his portfolio, is under-
stood to have lost all interest in the case, Taylor,
for his part, considers that further discussion is to
be deprecated. The Ministerial “ amen ” has been
received with “hear, hear,” by the directors of
the Land Credit Bank, and everybody seems
satisfied except the smaller shareholders and
depositors, who are too dead-broke to be worth
considering. As for the odd million or so of
Victorian public, they have now learned to think
of G. N. Taylor’s two years as a representative
joke, and would be sorry to have the humour of
the situation spoilt by another trial.
Let that case of G. N. Taylor’s
Fade away from view ;
Leave him joking with his gaolers
Whilst the weepers and the wallers
(Who are not a few)
Curse the bank completely busted,
Curse the man they once entrusted
With the gold for which he lusted,
Curse and groan and look disgusted—
What else can they do ?
Hundred thousand golden thick Tins
Vanished at a pop !
Though a two years’ sentence sickens
Those who wonder why the dickens
Taylor cannot swap
Places with the dupes that sadly
Roam at large, protesting madly
They would “ do his bit,” and gladly,
For the “ stuff ” they want so badly—
Let the matter drop.

For a Pick-me-up in the morning try a “ milk
punch” made at Walsh’s. New American Bar,
Sydney Theatre Royal Hotel.
It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is
just the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*
Almost!
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Well, that shed is cut out, and here I am, on the
toad again to look for another.
It wasn’t such a bad shed, after all. I took
fifteen pounds out of it —would have been twenty-
five only for my wrist caving in and throwing me
idle for a fortnight. It served methe same trick
when I was shearing on Bardeggie, three years
ago. However, there’s some of the season left yet,
and if I can drop in at Muldilla I’ll be right
enough.
Let’s see—what’s its name again ? Muldoon—
no—Muldilla. Ah ! that’s it. I always had a
poor memory for names.
Whew ! It is hot, and, by Jove, the water-bag
is getting light. I must stop this smoking; it
makes me too thirsty.
Only seven miles done out of twenty-seven, and
the water nearly all gone. The bag’s too small,
anyhow.
I shall have to make another one as soon as 1
can get a bigger piece of canvas. I daresay
there’ll be some at Muldilla.
A bit of a spell wouldn’t go bad now. This
swag is beginning to fit pretty tight on my
shoulder. _
Hum; I can’t sit in this flaming sun, and if Igo
into the shade of one of those trees, it’ll be swarm-
ing with ants ; any bit of shade always is.
Oh, here’s a log under this bush. I’ll camp on
it for a bit.
My word, it’s a relief to get rid of the swag; it’s
infernally heavy. I’ll lighten it to-morrow, if I
don’t get any shearing at the next place.
Well, I suppose I must push on again ; there’s a
good few miles yet to do.
I seem to be getting into fresh country; the
road’s getting rather rocky.
It’s a bit rough on the feet, after coming out of
a woolshed, which always makes ’em soft for out-
side work.
Hullo ! Where’s the road gone to now ! I can
hardly pick it out among these boulders.
There’s some wheel-tracks going this way. But
ithere, it doesn’t matter which I follow ; the road
■will show up again when I get through this stony
;ground. .
Confound it! I can’t see any sign of it at all
now. Never mind ;it must be out this way some-
where, so I’ll shove on and chance it.
Where the deuce can it have gone to ! This will
never do. I must have come a mile and a half
since the last track disappeared. I shall be
;getting into trouble if I don’t look out. I’ll try
back.
Let’s see. I fancy I came this way. No—l
never passed that big black rock, I’ll swear. The
road ought to be more to the right, I think.
It’s not this way—oh, of course not —it’s away
tto the left. What a thickhead lam !
Still no sign of it! It can’t be this way or I
should have fetched it before now.
Let me think a bit. Oh, it’s no use getting
frightened ; the road can’t be far away.
It ought to be down here, by the sun ; but
that’s not much of a guide for a job of this sort;
and it’s shifted a good bit while I’ve been
muddling about here. I’ll have a drink and drive
on down this way anyhow.
What, all the water gone? Then I must find
that road somehow, or else this heat will soon
ripen a rare old thirst for my benefit!
It’s no good—there’s no road this way and—by
God, I’m lost I
What am I to do now I I’ll lighten the swag
and try to strike a bee-line out of here. Now,
what shall I part with ? The stone can go ; I can
easily get-another when I’ve got some shears to
sharpen. The boots can go too, and I might as
well leave the mat. There, that makes it lighter ;
and now for another try to get somewhere.
I wish to heaven I had a drink !
Shall I never get out of this infernal bush?
There goes the sun down ; it’ll get a bit cooler
now.
My feet are blistering awfully—but I must push
on while there’s light.
It’s getting too dark to go on any farther to-
night—l shall have to camp down for an hour or
two.
How horrible the thirst is getting, and its
going to be a roasting hot night.
Ah, well, I’ll try and get a little sleep, and per-
haps in the morning
Heigh ho ! what a dreadful dream ! How real
it seemed ! Why* the pangs of Ah, by God,
it’s no dream, it is a reality ! lam lost!
Unless I get water soon 1 shall be in trouble.
No—l won’t take the swag ; quite enough to do
to take myself, for I must make a big try to reach
some place where I can get a drink before things
get too bad.
I’ve heard that it is a good thing to put a stone
in the mouth. I’ll try it.
It is an improvement, I believe. And now,
here’s off.
That sun’s flaming hot again ! It must be
getting on for midday. I wonder how much
longer this muddle’s going to last?
Ah ! what’s this ? A break in the bush.
Hurrah ! the open country at last —and yes—
by Jove! —there’s water—l can see it quite plain
what a fine big sheet of it, and how it glitters
in the sun ! .
Whew ! It’s —no—good—trying to run to it—it
is farther off than I thought. And yet—it can’t
be so far either, for I can plainly hear it rippling.
How my head reels! Never felt like this
before.
It’s queer—l don’t seem to get any closer to
that water ! It’s always shifting away from me—
and there goes the sun down !
Well, dark or light, I must push on, I know
where the water lies and if I follow.it, I am bound
to strike it some time.
Hullo ! a fall—l must be more cautious in this
darkness or I’ll come to grief.
What’s that ? Some one on that knob. Hey !
Oh, it’s only a kangaroo. Things are very
deceiving at night time.
It’s an awfully close night! It’ll be a stinger
when the sun gets up.
Hush ! Is that a crow ? If it is I must be near
water. Pish ! What am I talking about ?—crows
don’t sing out at night. It’s some night bird I
expect.
Down again ! My head caught it that time.
How’s this ? Wet! Why—oh, I must have cut
myself.
I ought to soon reach water now. I’ve come a
long way during the night.
There’s daylight again. I’ll be able to get on
better now.
Lord, how my head aches, and how redevery-
thing seems! I can’t see any water now, either !
I expect I turned away from it in the darkness.
Never mind, I must come to some place soon.
Something out there looks like the mullock out
of a tank. I hope to God it is !
Only a sandridge I Christ 1 shall I never find
water ?
How that sun is biting agaiil t I’ll take off my
waistcoat and hahg it up in this tree. I can find
it again when I want it. ;
My word ! Look at those fountains ! How
they sparkle ! ! I’m right at last.
I can’t make this out —they’re all gone again !
But what’s this? Can it be—ah, it’s only
another of those sandridges.
. Listen. How long have I been lost ? No —it
can’t be more than a week, because it’s only been
dark
I must get back home next week. Jenny will
give me all the water
Water ! Why, here’s plenty of water —look at
it all round ! I’ll strip and then I can plunge in.
I can beat-all the boys at swimming !
The teacher says if he were — When Muldilla’s
cut out, I’ll make in for the river. Why, here’s
the river ! It’s queer I can’t reach it! If Jenny
were only ,
I wonder what makes my head burn so! There s
something through the scrub yonder. Another of
those sandridges, I expect. No, I won’t go over
to it, anyhow. I’ll push on to
Muldilla, Jan. 12,18
To the Constable-ih-charge, Todooma.
Dear Sir,—l beg to report that, on Thursday last, while
riding through the No. 4 paddock on this run, I found the
dead body of a man lying in a thin patch of scrub, about
three hundred y-ards from Watson’s tank. The body was
quite nakfcd, and appeared much bruised, especially’ about
the head.
If you will call at the station, you can get directions to
my hut, from which I shall be ready to conduct y T ou to
where the body is lying.—Yours.
WALTER RICHARDS, Boundart Rider.
To Readers of “The Bulletin.”
“Every man can write at least one book,” every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man With or without brains moves in
a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word ; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gets the guileless public to work for him for noth-
ing. Short stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting, social, or dramatic themes, are
preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words uo to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better. Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-colunm where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
Suggestions for illustration with or without
rough explanatory drawings will be gladly re-
ceived by The Bulletin.
A.D. 1950.
And thus Australia —while about her feet her sons,
Furrow-brow’d and sad, crouched listening,
Her voice, where love with anger fought, shivered
To the mountain tops, and curious stars came out
To see men take their dose of Truth.
“ You ask, my sons, why life is sad and why
Old age with giant’s pace o’ertakes
Mid-way ’twixt birth and death ;
Why greed stalks rampant through the land,
Spoiling youth ; and laughter, satire-soaked,
Comes from the birds alone.
Listen ! yonder in the distant days while Fate
Went kissing Northern lands, I lay
And nursed my barrenness, storing
Little hoards of gold away, as old maid will,
Pining for child —ever to the Southern breeze I said :
“ I’ll wait, I’ll wait, men must come, and I,
Welcoming, will laugh in harvest —for God is good.
They came—your sires, and saw, not my flowers.
Nor the rich breasts of me—bursting to feed,
But ripped my robe, and through all chastity.
Made for an old maid’s savings.
One night, while busy sleep men rocked
In the rigor of their dreams, the southern wind,
My old-time friend, crept back, and I—
“ What name give they this mate of mine ?” .
And he, whispering low, “They call him ‘avetice, his lote'
Begets his like—thy little store misspent,
They, greed-impelled, shall on each other feed.
I have no tears for you, nor further word than this,
Had I with effort mated and bred our brawny sons
Owning stocks, nor shares, nor wanting them,
This fair earth had known no land so fair.
But now, farewell —go burrow on as vermin will.
And when the gold is gone come back to me,
Repentance-led, and 1 have golden corn for you,
Laughing vines, and sweet forgiveness.
MANNINGTON CAFFYN.
“ I lost £500 through the public discover--
ing that I sold goatsflesh instead of lamb,
coolly deposed an insolvent in Melbourne Court,' <
the other day, and the boys yelled “Baa!
after him. This man did business in a very large ■

The supers, at Sydney Royal have been 1
uttering a gentle wail against the employment on 1
the stage of a party of N.S.W. regular artillery-
men. It isn’t often that the super, kicks. He lias ■
generally been climbing life’s ladder the wrong
way, and hasn’t got spirit enough left to buck.-
Still there are exceptions. When a certain Aus-
tralian governor visited “Siberia” at a certain;
Australian theatre lately, sundry supes were told
off to stand outside the theatre in livery. One of
them objected. He didn’t mind figuring as a-
Tommy Atkins, but his democratic soul drew the’
line at flunkeys, so he was “sacked” instanter.
Years ago, too, there was that heroic member of
the guild who smote Dondi, the basso, at Mel-
bourne Opera House. The genial Enrico had
sundry little stage mannerisms which this profane-
wight used to mimic behind the scenes. Dondi 1
caught him at it and kicked him in the back-
blocks, whereupon the valiant supe went for him>
like a whirlwind. There was a general kick-up.
Rosnati fled the scene, and Lucy Chambers, fear-
ing for dear Henrv, rushed across to the police-
station in Little Collins-street. Lyster cursed as ;
only he could curse, and Gagli tore his hyacinthme
locks, but when it was all over Dondi, like the’
trump he was, took all the blame, and this paladin 1
of supers, wasn’t dismissed after all.

The Eight-Mile Tragedy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Eight-Mile” was one of the finest sheets
of water on the run on the river for that matter,
for it was one of the dry, inland rivers in Western
Queensland wherein the water-holes are few and
far between. It was a warm part of the world,
and in summer the broad plains through which
the many-channelled stream wound its way were
full of gaping holes and cracks, and the hot winds
came across them like blasts from a stoke-hole.
The Eight-Mile was a favourite camping-place,
and, induced by this consideration, an individual
proceeded to erect a rough accommodation house
there. He was a pallid, unhealthy-looking man,
whose pasty face contrasted strangely with the
sun-blackened complexions of the station men.
and he had an unhealthy-looking* wife and was-
accompanied by an equally unhealthy-looking
rouseabout, who assisted in the erection of the
accommodation house
ihe prejudice of the district was decidedly
against new-comers; as one of the men
‘they looked too much like so many
kopsuses, but as they all proved quiet, kept a
decent table, and were not much suspected of
either sly-grog selling or horse-stealing, they rose
in public estimation, and when Butler (for
that \\ as the man s name) enlarged his habitation
and applied for a publican’s license, it was granted
without much trouble. Naturally things altered
somewhat, limothy J. Butler was rather more
self-assertive when lording it behind the bar,
and the rouseabout, promoted to be barman sud-
denly developed a sporting, horsey vein hitherto
unsuspected. Still Timothy B. retained his credit.
No outrageous cases of Ihocussmg or lambing-down
were ever reported, and as he now kept a Chinese
cook and gardener and spared no pains to
provide good meals, his place was well patronised
and prosperity appeared to loom ahead.
Mrs. Butler, however, was still a woman of
mystery. limothy and the promoted barman
grew browner in face and more genial in their
manner, but she still remained pale, silent and
reserved. If addressed she answered in the
fewest possible words, and took the first oppor-
tunity of leav mg the room.
One afternoon there was a goodly number of
people assembled at Tim.’s, as the place was now
known. Three teams Were camped at the water-
hole, some of the men from the neighbouring-
station were down, and a traveller or two were
staying there. A few of the boys were playing;
quoits with horse-shoes, some were pitching com-
petition yarns, and the balance talking horse with
the barman. At this juncture a stranger rode up
to the place. He was a big-boned, black-bearded
man with enormous feet and hands. He was;
riding a miserably poor, knocked-up horse which
fairly staggered when his rider dismounted, with
some difficulty extricating his large feet from the
stirrups.
Mrs. Butler was just coming into the verandah
when the new-comer alighted, but at sight of him
she beat a hasty retreat. “ Good day to yez all,” 1
said the stranger in a rich brogue, as he
glanced around, and then, as Butler appeared, he
gave a shout, and exclaimed: “And ’tis yerself,
Tim, ould man, after all, then !” ‘
Tim, however, did not appear to reciprocate the
effusive greeting of his friend, but he treated him
eivilly, sent the black boy to hobble the wretched!
horse out on the nearest bit of feed, and asked his;
guest in. The stranger drew one of his hug©
hands across his mouth and followed with
alacrity. Public opinion in the verandah decided
that he was an ex-policeman and a new-chum in
the bush.
In the course of the afternoon Tim mentioned
the stranger as his brother-in-law, John Dwyer,
It was noticeable that he spoke of him as John,
not the more familiar Jack, although Dwyer
affectionately dubbed him Tim at every oppor-
tunity. Mrs. Butler did not appear as usual to
dispense the tea from the big tin teapot at the
evening meal, and the only person who seemed
elated at the visit was Dwyer himself. He was
hi high good-humour, “shouted” for everybody
wll ° would drink with him, as though the place
belonged to him ; and, after an uproarious carouse,
fell asleep with his head under the table and his
legs dangling over a form.
From that time forth Tim’s was an altered
place. Dwyer, who appeared to be in his proper
element, lolled behind the bar from morning till
night, and his broad fingers were continually
clutching a whisky-bottle. Trade fell off,
for his assertive ways disgusted everybody,
liigh and low, master and man, and much
wonder was expressed that Butler stood it. There
came an end, however, to his patience. It was
soon rumoured that Butler wanted to sell out, and
as the district had increased in importance during
his sojourn in it, he was not long without a suit-
able offer. He accepted it, and it was soon known
that Mr. Dwyer had departed without beat of
drum. Shortly afterwards the Butlers and their
faithful henchman left, and the new people
entered into possession.
Three months had elapsed since Tim’s-by
w hieh name it was, and probably is still, known-
;had changed hands, and three or four teams had
turned out at the Eight-Mile to camp. It was
only about 2 0 - clock) and the black boy, bucket and
billy in hand, went down to the water-hole to
bring up water. The men who were unyoking
were suddenly startled by flying footsteps? and a
scared nigger rushed into their midst with the
astounding assertion that an alligator was in the
water-hole.
Big Bob elevated his tall, sinewy frame, six
foot four in his bare feet, and accompanied by his
mates strode down the track. On the surface of
the water floated a remarkable-looking object.
The men gazed in silence for some time,
“If it was possible,” said Big Bob at last, “I
should say those were Dwyer’s feet.” He flicked
his bullock whip at it, but it fell short. “Lend
us a lash,” he said.
One of the men unbent his whip from the
keeper and handed it to him. He fastened it on
to his own, made a noose at the end, and with one
skilful east sent it over the strange object and
towed it ashore.
• It was Dwyer’s feet, and there was a body
attached to it. A horrible body, so bloated in
places, so shrunken in others, so mutilated by fish,
so unlike anything human, and yet still bearing a
horrid likeness of humanity about it, that some of
the men retired,
With the aid of another whip, Big Bob and two
or three others got the thing on to the bank. A
bag full of stones had been tied to it, but the fas-
tenings had parted and the corpse had come to the
surface.
“ We camped here a fortnight ago,” said Big
Bob, looking at the others,
“ Yes,” was the reply. They were on the up-
trip then, now they were returning empty,
“ And,” said Bob, solemnly, “ we made our tea
and our bread of this water, and boiled our beef
in it. By , if ever I catch Butler, I’ll —I’ll—
Words failed him here.
Everybody put it down to Butlerbut no trace
of him could be discovered. No marks of violence
were found on the body ; Dwyer’s hands and feet
had been tied together, probably when he was in a
drunken stupor, and so he had been chucked into
the waterhole.
“ I’ll never forgive himsaid Big Bob.
“ Why the devil didn’t he bury him ?”
“Too many niggers about,” replied his mate.
“Bound to have tracked him up out of sheer
curiosity.” Which was true, as a camp of blacks
had established themselves near the place.
There were some startling telegrams in the
metropolitan papers, and a verdict of wilful
murder against Timothy Butler ; but that was all.
I was jackerooing on the next station, at the
time, and ten years afterwards accidentally heard
the “ rights ” of Dwyer’s fate.
It came about in this way. I was in Sydney
walking down George-streot; at that time the
totalisators were in full and open swing, and one
fellow who was standing in the street taking a
customer’s name, was knocked down by a cab. I
recognised his white face as they lifted him up ; it
was Butler’s barman. I called at the hospital next
morning and found that he had been badly injured
and could not possibly live. He recognised mo,
and presently told me about Dwyer.
Butler and he, it seems, had been mixed up in a
swindling racing transaction, which, connected as
it was with the suspicious death of a man, made a
great stir at the time.
Butler and the ex-barman got eight years over
it. Mrs. Butler had secreted most of the money,
and, by bribing Dwyer, then a warder, the two
escapee}. They made out back to the most
uncivilised part they could find, and were doing
well by honest means when Dwyer, who had lost
his billet, tracked them down and established
himself at free quarters. He was ruining the
business and Butler, despairing of ever getting
free from his incubus, got rid of him in the way
mentioned. The Butlers, it seems, had since gone
to America, and their accomplice died a few days
after he told me the story.
D.
Archie Brown, the brother and successor
to that John whose body lies a-mouldering under a
huge monument, is the present autocrat of the
Queen’s whisky-bottle. Every night, on the stroke
of half-past ten, poor dear John’s brother bursts
upon the royal scene bearing a decanter of whusky,
a water-bottle, and a tumbler upon a silver
charger, and administers a certain fixed dose to
Her Majesty. She always says, with an air of
monarchial surprise— “ What! is it really time for
me to take my whisky, Brown?” and Archie in-
variably replies, “What are you giving us, Your
Majesty ” (or words to that effect), and the
Crowned Head takes the mixture as before.
Archie Brown plays the same “ sturdy independ-
ence ” game-as John used to play, and regulates
his Sovereign Lady’s refreshments without seem-
ing to consider whether there arc any kings
present.

The new Governor of Maorflancl has
requested that all the drains at Goverfunenx
House, Wellington, be repaired and the p] aer ,
disinfected as much as possible,before his arrival
Evidently he has heard evil reports' about the
malarious city, and is coining out with fear and
trembling. Still he ought to like the place when
he gets there, especially on Sunday, for-barring
Dunedin—Wellington looks more horribly Scotch
on the day of rest than any other locality we ever
visited. The present writer first struck it on a
Sunday, and the experience will 'never, never be
forgotten. It was two o’clock on a sodden after
noon when he banged at the door of a hotel, and
was let in under protest by a man whose religion
seemed to have flown to his head. After securing a
bedroom he was turned adrift in a big, hare
parlour, and left there to amuse himself till
tea-time. The shutters were up, but there were
holes in the tops of them which admitted a faint
yellow glimmer like a corpse-light. There was no
one else in sight, in fact there appeared to ho no
one else in the house. He asked for a paper, and
got half a one—the half with the advertisements
in it. Then he roamed wretchedly around the
big, brown, desolate apartment in search of books
and found a volume upon Napoleon’s retreat from
Moscow. He studied that .subject for four solid
hours till a haggard girl came, and in a sepulchral
voice said, “Tea!” At the tea-table there
were six people sitting straight up in grim,
silence, like the spectres of six dead, wet
Sabbaths. They wolfed the provender hurriedly
and retired apparently to bed. Then the
writer went back to the empty parlour, and
read the x’etreat from Moscow straight ahead till
11 p.m. At that hour the haggard girl put her
head in at the door and asked if he wanted any-
thing more that night. He said he did—he wanted!
a bottle of poison, two razors, a pistol, a liang-
maii’s rope, and the loan of a cemetery.- The
haggard girl fled in horror on hearing this, and he>
gathered up the book and went upstairs, and
crawled wretchedly into bed. Being unable to
sleep he lay there and listened to the rain, and
read the retreat from Moscow till four a.tn., where
the candle flickered out, after which he lay in
the dark and thought over the retreat from;
Moscow. About five he went to sleep, and!
dreamt that he had discovered an entirely new
way of running a newspaper so that there were no>
expenses, and all the takings were clear profit. If
Lord Glasgow only strikes Wellington on a good,,
old, damp, solemn Sabbath, he will baa contented!
Scotchman and the happiest Presbyterian goinm
An average Presbyterian’s idea of happiness con-
sists in reading the Shorter Catechism in a ceme-
tery, and Wellington is nearly all cemetery or
thereabouts.

(More or less translated from the French.)
“ No !’* he said, sadly but firmly, “ 1 cannot and
will not eat cold boiled mutton for lunch,*’
She faced him equally resolute, but beautiful as
ever. “ Then earn more than £2 a week,”
When a woman taunts she is ctlleh Peter
Would gladly have earned more than 40 shillings
Weekly, but in these times of depression it was
hot possible. He sighed deeply and murmured
the word “ pickles ” ?
“Go,” she said, with a superb gesture, “and
get your lunch where you cari.”
He went, and with him LoVe passed Out of the
little cottage and Discord entered;
He took the train .into town arid nursed hi 4
Wrath as jt rattled ojiward. “Yes,” he said, as
tie refiecteq, that he still lO shillings in his
pocket, lor pt was the peginning of the w,eek j “I
will lunch at Paris House,” aqd h,e did so. i
~ He had an, artistic, lunph, then he met a frien,d
and they went and had a whisky grog together.
Another whisky grog followed, and then they met
another friend who also would shout whisky grog.
It was sublime, the afternoon passed like a dream.
When they sallied forth it was dark and the lamps
were alight.
Left to herself, Felise stood for a few moments
with a smile frozen on her lips, then she gazed
scornfully at the cold mutton, and with a gesture
of disdain swept from the room.
Felise had a stocking. Ido not mean the hose
she was wearing, but a secret hoard of money,
small, but at the present juncture, available. I
will go in to town and get my lunch, she thought,
perhaps I shall meet my old sweetheart, Jaques,
and he will take me to the theatre.
Peter felt like making a night of it—whisky
grog makes one amorous. “ I will go to the theatre
and see the burlesque,” he meditated, He went,
but when he reached the pay-office he was unable
to find the requisite coin, Horror ! but then
whisky grog costs money. He turned and con-
fronted a lady and gentleman, sdcre-e-b )!! it Vvas
Felise and his former rival, JaqUeS; With si yell
of fury he hurled himself upon then!, and theil all
was chaos.

A Chinaman was found dead, a month or
two ago, in a lonesome part of West Australia,
ifnd as he looked as if he had been pounded to
death with steam hammers; it was set down as a
case of murder. So three other Chinamen were
arrested, the policeman, as he ran them into the
“logs,’’ warning them as usual that any state ;
tnents they made might bo used against them.
As they didn’t knoW a Word df English; afad thd
billy statements! they made were “ Shdbdi-ki-la,”
this wilrning was rather misplaced; perhaps, but it
didn’t uiatter. There were only two lawyers for
feoriie hundreds of miles around, so One of them
got a commission as acting - judge, and the
other was appointed Crown - prosecutor, and
there was no lawyer left for the unhappy
Chows. However, the Court hunted up
ah interpreter who was ordered to explain to the
prisoners “ the nature of the accusation,” but it
presently transpired that the interpreter knew no
English himself, and hadn’t the faintest idea what
the nature of the accusation was, and consequently
couldn’t explain it, so the court decided to go
ahead without him. The prisoners were asked, in
English, how they would plead, and as they
remarked “Chow-wow,” or words to that effect,
it was resolved to set them down as pleading
“not guilty,” The Crown prosecutor opened the
case, and called his witnesses, and the prisoners,
being asked, also in English, if they wished to
cross-examine, again answered sadly “ Chow-
wow.” Then the Crown prosecutor delivered his
address to the jury, and the prisoners were
invited —in English—to open the case for the
defence. Sadly, stolidly, and unmeaningly they
responded “ Chow-wow.” Whereupon the jury
brought in a verdict of “Guilty” without the
slightest hesitation, and the acting-judge asked—
in English, as already mentioned—whether they
could show any cause why sentence of death
should not be passed upon them, and they all
three rose up with one accord and made this brief,
memorable statement, “Chow-wow!” After
that the judge sentenced them in due form, and
the Executive, a week or two later, confirmed the
sentence, and the three Mongols will m, hung in
the ordinary course.

Despite the learned opinion of Sydney
Evening News to the contrary, the abbreviation
“ Mons.” is quite allowable, and, in the interests
of comprehensibility, is preferably allowable in a
programme printed jil English. In French docu-
ments where the full hame of the person denoted
•is usually spelt right out after the prefix denoting
“Monsieur,” the abbreviation “M.”— the equiva-
lent of the English “Mr.” —is all right enough ;
but, with the English vogue of initialling name's,
the “M.” would often be misunderstood to repre-
sent a Christian name, such as Michael, Matthew,
Mark, Melchisedeck, Moses, Marius, and others.
The Evening News is only an authority on police
“ langwidge.’’

The public eye rejects as uninteresting mon
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day, It is only the remainder that is found
in Thu BULLETIN,
The Bulletin is an aggressive Bemomtio
papet tvhich strives to exclude from its artist p
oally-boilciensed CbllrmilS all matter which is not
of general human interest, The Bulletin was
started eleven feats ago with flo capital but brains,
ar-d has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have nnavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more Or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its_ lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparin'*
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands bf pounds in fitting their buildings
with thfe latest appliances i(Jt high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed With hew type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, arid , aidotried With
sketches ftom the bands of the ablest arid best-
paid rlewspaper artists Australia has rivrit serin.-
The Bulletin is the only well-established .Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
THE Bulletin Is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land. Wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity,
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, aerainst the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, Tint
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Ear North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maoriland
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the bnghtjs
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and mos,
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.
